RATING: 6
London didn't hit the mark for me. I cannot say that it missed the goal completely but it's just nowhere near the level I would require from a boardgame for it to find its way into my shelf. So London isn't a bad game, only so-so.

The reason for this was in the cards. It just doesn't make sense to me why to have two identical cards when for the other one you have to fulfill a prerequisite for using. That just makes the worse cards useless.

I also found the I'll slap your face cards totally artificial. To me, they look like a blatant attemp to introduce interaction to the the game.

Surprisingly for an eurogame the theme was maybe a little more present than what's usual.

RATING: 9
"No, Sir, when a man is tired of London, he is tired of life; for there is in London all that life can afford."  (Samuel Johnson)

London the game is a heavy card game, with economic and area-control elements.  It's a pretty great game, and my only concern is long-term legs (less variability here than in, say, Jambo).

Good points:  the theme shines through.  The graphics are subtle but nice, and the card names and functions combine with the overall mechanisms to really evoke London's history if you like that kind of thing (I do).  Want a capitalist dystopia, Dickens-nightmare of workhouses and poverty but lots and lots of cash?  Can do.  You can educate the poor if you want, though, and elevate the ton of your city (or City).  The south bank is different from the North.  The underground matters, etc.

The second nice thing (though this ties in to the bookkeeping problem) is that YOU GET TO RUN YOUR CARDS.  Most "build stuff" card games I've played end about when an engine runs.  This game starts you out with no money, lots of poverty, and little going on; by the end, you can churn our massive VPs or cube reductions or money-making or underground building, and feel like you really did something.  In that it's like a more controlled Glory to Rome.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
First off, I'm not a fan of card games, and London is most definitely a card game!
Secondly, London is commonly criticized as being multi-player solitaire, and a "rinse-and-repeat" game. It's not, on either account. In fact (on the first charge), it's downright cutthroat at times!
The artwork and general quality of the cards is fabulous: it almost makes me wish I liked card games! The board, however, is fairly drab, like in Mr. Wallace's "Princes of the Renaissance". But London, you're no Princes of the Renaissance.
I was foolishly expecting a sort of London-civ building game, and it's not at all a civ game. It's a card game, pure and simple. While the theming of the cards is sound, it just doesn't feel like you're building up London over the centuries.
I had read somewhere that the hand management was kind of an afterthought on Mr. Wallace's part, and that he had originally intended London as a tile laying game. I would have MUCH preferred it that way, without the excessive fiddliness of hand management that we get in this incarnation. 
And now, I must wait for someone (perhaps even Mr. Wallace himself) to come up with a true London civ building game for me. London was not an unmitigated disaster for me, but it was a considerable disappointment.

RATING: 8.5
Excellent abstract tableau builder, that lends more than a little thematic rational to the actions you're achieving (e.g. using a pauper card to pay for the placement of a Blue monument by "sending them to school" -- i.e. using the ability of the school card to alter the rule that normally prevents paupers from being used in such a manner).

Wonderful balancing acts are demanded by the poverty mechanic -- before play I wasn't aware how much of the game hinged on the application of this. Demands the 'Ben-Luca' variant to play well with 2, but a quick search on the Geek will illuminate you on those particulars. 

Note, only 2 games under my belt, both at the 2 player count. Excellent experience, despite being destroyed in both games by my partner. :) 

Only negatives are the components: they're a bit cheap. We quickly replaced the money included, and I'd love to find wooden replacements for the building tiles.

RATING: 3
2-4(3)

2p variant (Ben-Luca, recommended by UnknownParkerBrother). I'm not sure about this quite yet. There are plenty of interesting cards here, and there are several interesting dynamics to manage. You've got to handle your poverty, build up an engine (both pounds and VP), get boroughs and underground tokens, and try not to leave juicy cards out for your opponent.

It plays reasonably fast, with a card draw and a single action on a turn (sometimes we forget the card draw, and sometimes we forget we are limited to a single action on a turn) and the mechanisms are pretty well integrated. Well, I don't know, it still felt long for what it was (even with the neutral player speeding things along) and was thematically thin. Innovation "feels" thematic (even if it's just cardplay and the components don't convey a civ feel), RftG feels "sort of" thematic, but this somehow doesn't.

Also, turns feel sort of forced in that you almost know what you "ought" to do. Gee, I guess I better get a borough. I've got lots of cards so I guess I have to build. I just built all these cards, time to activate. Sure, this happens with RftG, San Juan, Innovation, etc. but ... well ... let's give it some more goes, shall we?

RATING: 6
Comparisons to Race for the Galaxy are almost inevitable, since this is Martin Wallace's take on the build a tableau, pay for cards with cards game.  So, imagine a game like RftG in which turns are consecutive rather than simultaneous.  Imagine the down time with four players. Now imagine a game like RftG in which the card effects are much blander; they all make money, earn vps, get rid of poverty cubes or some combination of the three.  

In London, the game lies in balancing your needs for money and vps, while minimising your poverty.  That's actually more subtle and interesting than it sounds.  Blind card draws and the rate at which the deck runs out add a degree of uncertainty. As with RftG there is little player interaction.  It mostly amounts to taking care not to discard cards of high value to others, although there are one or two mild take that effects as well. 

Overall, there is some interest in the game, but for me it feels a bit over-extended; compare a 90 minute stroll through London to the 30 minute warp speed of RftG.

Production quality is decent, although the cards are a bit thin.  The artwork is pleasing.

RATING: 7
Nice card game about the city of London. The game is  squeezed into its mechanics, which make it feel like some kind of bastard child of Race for the Galaxy and similar games - there's some added currency and anti-VPs (poverty points), and most of the cards are one use only, along with some more twists, but it's essentially pay cards to play cards to run an engine. 

But while these mechanics make the theme feel pasted, what gives true flavour to the game are the different cards, which are quite evocative of the city. The boroughs you buy are also a nice addition.

Typically for these card engine games, it plays pretty fast and usually with little downtime, providing lots of variability because of all the different cards. All in all, a pretty nice game, though the end can feel a bit abrupt - the cards you'd prize most for the rest of the game then become essentially useless, and you might not even have the time to run your city one last time.

A nice advantage is that it plays pretty well with two: it's less tight (particularly in terms of buying boroughs), but it also flows faster with less downtime.

RATING: 8.5
+ Really good card game with a growth engine. You need to balance point, money and keep poverty low.
+ You get cards, build them and activate them, earning you points, money and poverty but then you need to start all over again because the activated cards are lost. 
+ An asset of the game is the three stacks of cards, the cards get better as you progress through the stack.
+ As always with a Martin Wallace game, the theme of building London is fairly integrated.

- You really need to play London a couple of times to get to know the mechanics and appreciate the game.

RATING: 7
Ez nagyon jó! Amolyan táblás kártyajátéknak mondanám. Vagy kártyákat szerzel, vagy leteszed őket, vagy aktiválod. Van egy logikus üteme a játéknak, arra vagyunk ösztönözve, hogy váltogatva hajtsuk végre az akciókat. Ha túl sok kártyánk van, nem érdemes többet szerezni (maximálva van), ha túl sok van kijátszva, érdemes aktiválni, stb. Nagyon ügyes húzás a szegénységi korlát, ami ráerősít arra, hogy nehéz legyen megtalálni az egyensúlyt. Hiába olvastam a játék előtt több embertől is, hogy a pénz a végére elfogyhat, mégis így jártam én is. És tök utolsó lettem. Ötletes játék, az első Wallace, amit volt szerencsém kipróbálni, állítólag nem annyira jellemző rá ez a játék, de ezzel együtt kíváncsivá tett, milyen lehet a többi játéka:)

RATING: 6
Decent first game, but I was wondering if there's one true path to victory, and the strategy forums here seem to indicate yes.

Update after three more games -- Building land is (of course) very good, but it's a race. The game is also quite different based on # of players.

RATING: 7
London is a very strange beast: it's both so Wallacy and so atypical for Wallace! It's undeniably very different from any other Wallace game...
It's essentially a hand management game, where you want at the same time more cards and less cards. There are many meaningful decisions, a tight economy and several different ways to score points.
The rules are quite easy to grasp (compared to other titles from the same author), the game is short enough, and it has great potential for replayability.

Update march 11, 2011: it finally hit the table with 3 players. It is undeniably a multiplayer solitaire, but the different paths for victory points and the agonizing decisions make London a true gem! Vote raised to 8. Looking forward to playing it with 4!

Update november 9, 2012: the loan strategy seems very predominant, especially with w-players. Vote downgraded to 7.

RATING: 9.25
One of my all time favorites. This game is fantastic. I love games where you get to "run your engine". You know, where you (usually) get a tableau of cards that do all sorts of awesome stuff and you can choose to do them all as your turn. London does exactly this. Of course, it is a Martin Wallace game, so there is going to be some suffering as well. Poverty is given out for numerous things, including how many stacks of cards your borough has. There are cards that get rid of Poverty as well. The new version without the board does not intrigue me at all. I think the board adds a nice tactical element that the new edition is missing. The game also scales really well.

RATING: 8
TN Game Days play, we had a teacher on hand and the pace was slow for our first game. This one started out thick and abstract, but came together for me while the first few players went. The elegance and multifaceted design that Martin Wallace has here needs to be seen first hand. The game progressed in weight of achievement, while not bogging down. and like all great games should, it teaches you something while it entertains. I will not soon forget my stay in London Town, and its surrounding boroughs, the many decisions of  each draw, each turn. Paying particular attention to my little corner of the world while only occasionally looking out at the greater good. The fancy shops, and new services that one can bring to a town, while balancing out the need to address poverty, is in itself a very fluid, dynamic, artful game, and should be played by any who seek excellence be set in front of them, both in task, and execution bravo!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A good and meaty but slow game that has some (small) similarities with Race for the Galaxy and Uruk: Weige der Zivilisation.  There is a lot of angst on each turn/decision because unlike RftG, most of the cards are good (i.e. few are crappy and easy to discard as "money" to build other cards) and you constantly have to put cards out for other players to see/pick up from.  The fact that you only really have one action per turn means the game is slow to develop and you need to play once to understand the flow of the game.  To me, this game feels more like a more complicated version of Uruk: Weige der Zivilisation rather than a sibling of RftG.

UPDATE 2014: I always seem to lose, but I like the game because it's so difficult to decide which cards to use as "payment" to build your buildings.  The fact that your payment cards become available for opponents to take and build is a simple concept but makes the choices tricky in a good way.  The luck of the card draws can be a turn-off when you need a specific color, but you can mitigate this to a certain extent if you play "toward" the card colors visible in the face-up tableau (many of which are opponents' discarded cards).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another strong Wallace game, this one sees players developing part of the city of London by playing cards in front of them or by buying regions on the map. There's a "loss cube" Automobile element called poverty which can be removed with certain cards and incurred by utilising cards in the players city (cards in front of them). You have to use city cards to get money and so on, so you have to use the city. The poverty aspect is somewhat countered by buying regions, so the mechanisms link together neatly. 

We didn't muster a complete idea of strategy on the first play, but it seems that buying regions of the map is good and too many poverty cubes is bad!

RATING: 9
Another Martin Wallace brain-burner - this time with more cards and less tiles. Patented interaction between loans, misery (make that poverty now), income and victory points. It is a hybrid combination of board game and a tableau building card game (reminiscent of San Juan or Race for the Galaxy). As such there is hand management, tableau building and board position to be aware of. In the end it comes down to the construction of the most efficient victory point engine. Already we are seeing folks claiming to have found the "yellow brick road." That happened with Brass didn't it? We'll see...

Trust Martin to take by now familiar elements and twist them into something unique. I had it ordered before I was able to play it and breathed a considerable sigh of relief when the game play proved quite engaging. I'm hoping the playing time will come down considerably with experience, as I expect this to fit into the the category of "challenging filler."

Rating (8) is preliminary based on a single play (four players).
++++++
A couple more plays in and the game is impressing us. I was particularly pleased to find that it makes a fine two player game. There can be a bit of a scripted feel at certain stages but it is too early for me to conclude anything from that yet since it may just be group think at work as we all learn together. Great fun so far - thanks again Martin!

Rating is up to 9!

RATING: 4
Provisional rating after 1 play. Not my sort of game - multiplayer solo, but with downtime (unlike say San Juan or RftG). Not sure why there are 2 currencies for cards in the game - you need to ditch a matching card to play a card (so cards are currency) but some cards also need cash to play too.

Overall, too long, largely unnecessary board (boroughs could have been cards), lack of interaction and the theme is paper thin - many of the cards feel the same even if they are named different (pitch a card, gain 6 cash 1 vp, or pitch a card gain 2 cash and 2 vp) ... just got bored and wanted it to end (and I won ...)

Rating might change if I play again, but in no rush to do so.

RATING: 8
Another Hit from Wallace here.
For Wallace a suprisingly very clean, streamlined and unfiddly design. 
Yes, the core of the game is a tactical multiplayer soltaire, but with some nice and enough elements of interaction. There are some dircet cards interaction to tame your oppositon, but the chance or threat to use the dicards of another player and the poverty mechanism are the spice in this game. 
Also is the London building theme well implemented.
Here some beginner tips:
Don`t lose sight of your or the others poverty! The penalty can be easily 30 pts (vs 20 to 60 pluspoints)
Keep your handsize down if you activate cards and to the end of the game.
Without boroughs it will be the hard way.
Don`t be afraid to use some loans.

RATING: 6
The game is heavier than San Juan, one of our favorites, but seems to offer choices for scoring, in addition to the negative score for poverty. It also has a flavor of Glen More (take the cards you want), but is its own game system. I don't know all the cards, but buying land is the seemingly key to victory, which limits the strategic options. Building in the city and poverty points are a consideration, but the card placement, for activation with a mind, should result in income to buy land in the city, which seems to be the key for the ultimate victory. Decent card/board game that has a one-dimensional approach to victory.

RATING: 7.5
Wallace comes up with a very clever card game.

The innovative play made the game quite dynamic. Really like the poverty mechanism in "running the city". 

The rule book is clearly written, the game is non-fiddly, and there aren't pages of rule exceptions. You'd never know it's a Wallace.

One potential concern is the end game. No one wants to end it and get stuck with a handful of cards. That particular end game mechanic is quite punishing and prolongs the game unnecessarily. Whoever ends the game can also potentially be a kingmaker. This one aspect prevents this game from being a great game.

The board and the bits are minimal accessaries. Paying 40 euros for a deck of 110 cards is a bit steep.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Our group concluded unanimously that it's simply not a game that holds our interest. I think it is being traded away and likely won't be played by our group again.

Perhaps we have been spoiled recently by well designed games like Navagador, Dominant Species, and Seven Wonders, but London feels flat and uninteresting to me. It is on completely the opposite end of the scale of brilliance that Age of Steam was (Age of Boroughs?).

Personal thoughts: I feel that the borough design (cost, incentives, card discard mechanic) is poorly thought out. Even if players are buying boroughs moderately to heavily (as the game mechanics reward), you have rounds of players picking up cards, throwing out cards, moving cards to discard piles, for the first part of the game. Yawn.

The game suffers from a sameness amongst far too many cards, where the decision of whether to play card A or B results in me thinking "who cares?". 2 VP and 6 bucks vs 8 bucks vs 1 VP and 5 bucks. Whatever. I won our last game by buying boroughs like mad and playing any paired up colors I had when I had 4 or 5 sets. I didn't even particularly care what I drew. Two browns? Play them. Drew an omnibus. Free money. Whee.

Then there are a few cards that are simply overpowered compared to everything else (omnibus, underground), particularly in games with fewer players. So, if you draw well and score 2 omnibuses in a 2 player game you get a huge (unbeatable perhaps as alexfrog's initial thread posits?) advantage.

Wallace needs new play testers.

RATING: 7.5

The game is semi-broken (the card rush), or the replay value is limited (your turn options are limited to 3 different actions, the board never changes).  I've played the game with 4 twice, which was much fun, and all parties involved liked the game a lot.  With 2, however, the game is even better, as you have much more control, and can dictate what cards your opponent has access to much more.  The theme is nice (Ashar, as the resident Anglophile, particularly likes it) and the components very well done.  Yes, this is not a game that is radically different every play.  Many have complained that the cards are not different enough.  Well, I don't have a problem with subtle, and the cards have different values at different player counts.  This is one of those games with fairly limited parameters, but within those parameters everything is quite elastic - you could win running your city every third turn, or running it twice in a game.  This is not a heavy Wallace game - indeed, this is a light-to-medium weight game, but Wallace's best of those.  It might fall from the 10 in time, but as of now, this is my favorite Wallace design since Brass (although he's coming on strong lately, as First Train to Nuremberg is also pretty great).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There is a nice Civ-feel to this game that I like a lot, especially because I'm a huge British history fan.  It reminded me a little of Through the Ages, though it's much lighter and the game plays much faster. I also think the design is really interesting: you both want and don't want cards, either in your hand or in your tableau, which means you both want and don't want to buy districts (because they make you draw cards). Buying smaller districts means you draw fewer cards, and thus take fewer poverty tokens, but they also earn you fewer victory points. Building more buildings helps you gain more wealth and points, but also can hurt you immensely if you have too many in front of you. The fun of the game lies in finding the right balance between these elements.

RATING: 8
One play left me a bit worried that this was a bit long for its weight but very much looking forward to more plays and I'm pleased that we've been able to fit them in.  Those plays have speeded it up and confirmed that it's mostly a question of familiarity with the cards - a perennial issue for all these 'rules on the cards' type games. As ever with Wallace, you have to balance the upsides against the downsides, with few choices being only 'good' and none of those easy options as lucrative as the others, which involve suffering something 'bad'. For me that adds a notch or two to what is, compared to most Wallaces, an otherwise straightforward game.

RATING: 8
My 1,000th game rated on BGG, woohoo, and what a great game to hit 1,000 with!

I really enjoyed this game of hand management.  Very difficult decisions throughout!  Should I go with few piles I can operate more often, or build up for some big city operations?  Do I discard this card or that card?  Keeping a good balance between generating income, scoring points, and keeping your poverty down is a tough balance!

I thought the board play felt a little tacked on, but it was still enjoyable to have that option available, even though it felt a little detached from the card play aspect.

In addition to just being fun to play, the game is also gorgeous, especially with the limited edition buildings, which amazingly are in a different shape for each color.  Very unexpected and over-the-top, and makes the board look that much cooler as the game progresses.

Overall, one of the simpler to learn Martin Wallace designs in quite a while; perhaps the fist that hasn't produced a bunch of questions after the first read through the rules.  However, despite the simple rules, there are all kinds of interesting decisions as I've come to expect with Martin's games.  If you like hand management type games, definitely don't pass this one up!

RATING: 7
Both my wife and I really wanted to like London, in part because we like Brass so much, in part because of the artwork, theme, and subtle thematic details. It seemed like a very good game for us. 

Unfortunately, our first game lacked cohesion and any sense of progress. We were just sort of taking actions because we had to do something, but we were making the same small circle of decisions at the end of the game as we were at the beginning. We mostly felt along for the ride rather than at the helm of our cities. By about 1/3 of the way through the deck in our first game, we were flush with money and VP, and poverty was easily sidestepped. Yet we still had to continue playing with no goal to motivate us other than to continue to amass money and VP. There was nothing to build toward, no target to aim for, and nothing that depended much on our previous choices. It was just, "Oh, this card gives me more money and VP than this other one. I guess I'll discard the latter to play the former." My wife went the last 1/3 of the game without accumulating a single poverty point (even based on cards in hand at the end of the game). She had purchased half the boroughs, had made enough money to pay back all of her loans and then some, and yet we still felt trapped in the draw-a-card, pick-an-action cycle.

And I think under the tedium of this forced, but somewhat meaningless decision-making, my wife declared the game "soul-crushing." (She meant it in the same sense that you or I might declare a three-hour game of The Settlers of Catan "soul-crushing").

A geekbuddy of mine had the following comment on Tikal II: The Lost Temple, and was amazed at how much I could relate to it based on my experiences with two-player London:
enzo622 wrote:
I mean, it's just too easy to do everything you want to do. ... So each turn is, "I go here for 5 points. Your turn. I go here for 6 points. Your turn. Etc." 

... We all just went to the rooms we hadn't each explored yet and got our obligatory points. After all that going 'round, I pulled out two bonus cards for 15 points for the win. I kinda felt bad in the last few turns knowing I was sitting on 15 points and watching the other players make their moves. It didn't matter much what they did and I knew it.

Our last 1/3 of the game was pretty much: play all the cards in your hand, run your city to accumulate money and VP, buy a borough that gives you a handful of cards, play all the cards in your hand, run your city to accumulate money and VP, buy a borough that gives you a handful of cards, play all the cards in your hand. I was pretty confident that I had more VP cards in my stacks than my wife (I took many more loans early on, whereas she tried to tread water with money-generating cards), so the continued play felt pretty meaningless, other than the hope of her drawing to some 15-point mega card that may or may not exist. 

There's little sense of accomplishment -- just loop after loop of playing cards, running cities, and buying districts. Most of the stuff ends up face down at the bottom of a stack, so I can't even feel proud of my monument or my schools. There are some things I would do differently next time, and places where I now see that I could be more efficient. But given that I was pretty clearly winning, there was no incentive to win better (especially in a learning game). 

Thus, I found the game to lack an emotional payoff -- the sense of accomplishment that should come with a building-themed game. Despite the designer's clear efforts to make each card appropriately thematic, there wasn't a sense of narrative or cohesion that made me feel like I was crafting a city. I didn't dislike the experience, but I liken it to playing Dominion by shuffling 3 copies of every card from every expansion into a single, large deck from which everyone draws a new hand each turn. It's just sort of odd. 

Our second game used a 2-player variant to shorten the game, but I've concluded that London just seems to lack the per-minute entertainment value for me. The whole game is building your city to run, but running the city really only happens about once every 20 minutes, and the payoff is a handful of VP chits and a few plastic money disks. Something about that just seems wrong. But, unlike my wife, at least I don't find it soul-crushing. 


Update:
As I’ve gained experience, [i]London[/i] has become a game that I’m increasingly enjoying.  I went ahead and upgraded to the limited edition, which has produced a noticeable atmospheric effect for the better.  The game, especially with two, still feels like something of a relaxing stroll, rather than a frantic competition, but I’m continually understanding the game’s subtleties more and more.

RATING: 6
The most interesting aspect of this game is managing your poverty. Also, it is fun to build up your city, run it, and then rebuild it over and and over again. The combos that you generate aren't spectacular, or even cool really, but it is fun to do it over and over again.

It does seem though, that the rhythm you want to get into is build city, run city, buy borough, repeat. Which, while fun now, will probably get stale relatively quickly. Oh yeah, and get Omnibus and street lights.

This is probably why most times if I want to engine build, I'd rather it either be dynamic, like Dominion, or over the top, like Glory to Rome.

The biggest downside to this game is that it probably works best with four, taking longer to play with fewer and being much looser.

Note, the game is not colour blind friendly, as we noticed when one of the players tried to pay for a pink card using a grey card. They looked identical to him. Now, that was solved by simply pointing out that all pauper cards were grey, but if someone couldn't differentiate between any of the other colours, there is absolutely nothing else on the cards to help them figure out which colour is which.

RATING: 5
I must have been nuts when I pre-ordered the limited edition. It's more than I'd typically spend on a game...certainly without playing it previously or reading the rules. 

Fortunately, this game is (for now) a solid 8, maybe more with more plays. I found the iconography and gameplay of Race for the Galaxy to be a bit confusing and lackluster. With London, sure there's text on the cards, but it's clearer what each card can do. On any given turn, you take four basic choices, but the various cards effects provide variety.

The game is a balance between money, VPs, and poverty. And, it's pretty tight. I really enjoyed my first game. It's a bit slow the first goaround, with everyone reading the cards in their hands, focused on their own little cities. 

There's probably a bit of solitaire to this game. 4 players might take too long, and 2 players might not provide enough tension on the board. But, I like how the theme comes through with the card types, and I enjoy the tough balance of resources.

Update: first two player game with my wife. Wasn't sure if she would like it, but after one play, she's definitely willing to try it again. A two player game is a bit "leisurely," with less tension on the board. It's more an extended exercise in optimization. But as a spouse game, that's not all that bad.

Update: after six plays, I traded it away in a math trade (for Claustrophobia). I like the theme, I like the cards. But ultimately, my knocks on the game are that two players offers not enough angst, and four players takes too long. It's a very solitaire experience. Those are ultimately the downfalls.

RATING: 7
:sugar: :( :) :D :D This is a tableau-building card game; the game on the board is of minimal importance and interest. (Though the purchase of boroughs is [i]highly[/i] important if you want to, well, win.)

A tough game to get a handle on: Race For The Galaxy is a more multi-dimensional and strategically rich game, Pax Porfiriana has a much richer theme and narrative drive, and Fleet is just a tighter and more focused design overall. London, while good, should by rights be a bit squeezed out.

But, I come back to London all the time, and I just enjoy it every go, and I don't think that's the nostalgia talking. Some of that is the presentation talking. The cards, with their hand-drawn images of Victoriana (and earlier, and later) stir a bit of Anglophilia I wasn't quite sure I had. And the game makes some hay out of a tight little rule set.

Money flows early and dries up late, so there's a bit of the old Dominion dynamic of setting up a money engine then concentrating on VPs later on. Most cards seem pretty balanced but in the best possible way -- they enable a good player with bad draws to overcome a bad player gifted with the good cards. There are a few combos (the dreaded double Omnibus leaps to mind, or an early draw of East India Company and Hospital) that are probably a bit overpowered.

Do you [i]need[/i] this if you're satisfied with your existing stash of tableau-builders? Probably not. But as of this writing, I've played this 12 times, it's the most recent game I've played, and if it was the [i]next[/i] game I played, I wouldn't be upset. That's some decent staying power.

RATING: 6
Copy/pasted from my discussion here:
http://boardgamegeek.com/article/7136875#7136875

I am finding that games with a large set of cards you can't possibly learn without experience that all do wacky and unpredictable stuff, especially when players have hidden hands of such cards, just don't resonate with me. Although I still find the game interesting, just not terribly fun.

My "dream" euro game is one where anything random and unseen is at least easily understood up front, and/or comes up in a way that it can be understood when it comes up, before it directly affects any single player positively or negatively. Games like Dominant Species on the long end, and Troyes and Lancaster on the shorter end (just to pick a couple recent games) exhibit this property. That is, I want to be able to "load" the whole FSM in my brain. Modifying the FSM midgame (like in Troyes) is also ok, so long as it affects everyone, and everyone sees it before it takes effect. If some of the variables which certain transitions depend on are unknown to me, I'm ok with that, but it's the structure that I want to understand. And while the game obviously has a high level FSM that is easy to understand (how to get cards and build boroughs), it's not at a level of detail that includes any of the important state changes like gaining money or losing poverty. (If people looked at my collection, they could probably find counter examples. It's possible that I'm not articulating this with pinpoint accuracy.) 

This game also falls into another category of games that I'm finding don't thrill me. It's a specific subcategory of multi-player solitaire: games where you can base your decisions on what other players are doing, but it's really hard to keep tabs on what the other players are doing. In games like Goa, players bump up against each other for a while (a good thing), then do their own stuff. And if you look at someone else's stuff, it's easy to parse their state . There is a set of variables, and you can see at a glance what those variables are (just look at their levels on their board, and resources on hand). In London, on the other hand, if you want to actually compete with your fellow players, rather than just enjoy building your little corner of the universe, you need to really pay attention to what cards they have in front of themselves. And for another example game: Merkator, although London is much worse in this regard, as Merkator's cards are all consistent and only vary by a small set of fixed parameters, and are also longer lasting. But the cards in London are hard to tell what they do at a glance (impossible from across the table, unless you already know the card and can read the title--art is generally too detailed to be easily recognizable as a cue). And it's even harder to tell how they interact together. Because they are all interconnected. Not to mention that in London a large portion of a players cards in front of them (or even all of them) can completely change in a single turn. So if you want to base your play on what your opponents are up to, you have to do a lot of un-fun work (based on my definition of fun, of course).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This is a tight economic game that involves the rebuilding of London after the great fire.  There are limited ways to make money but a lot of ways to spend it, so careful management is necessary.

The other key mechanic is poverty - every time you choose to activate your cards, you will likely pick up these dreaded points.  So your goal is to find ways to reduce those points as often as possible.

Once the mechanics are understood, this game zips along at a good pace.  Most of the components are very nice (especially the artwork on the cards), although the money chips are a disappointment (the limited edition has better wooden counters).

On the whole a great game from Martin Wallace that I expect will get lots of play in our house!

RATING: 7
Components: The board looks nice, and could only be improved by reserving a space in each borough for our building tiles. The backs of the cards should be more plain, to help differentiate them from face-up cards. The iconography is clear. The card images are interesting. Several cards are unambiguous, yet have clarifications in the rules that add an additional rule that we would never have thought to look for clarification on, because the card doesn't skirt the issue.

Experience: Tight budgeting game. I try to efficiently set up my city, then run it while my hand size is at a minimum and my borough buildings at a maximum. At the beginning money is easy to come by, but then suddenly it's very tight.

Like Agricola, the game seems to want me to dabble in everything. I'll be interested to see if strategies exist that focus on just a few aspects of the game (e.g. just on building in boroughs, or just on high-VP cards, etc.). Going through the entire deck each time might become a bit repetitive, but I'm eager to dive back in.

RATING: 6
[6 Oct 2017] - 6 (6 plays)
Despite the rating, I do really enjoy this game. The dings against the game are really highlighted in the changes that the second edition made. The original had some very scripted opening moves - take loans, buy boroughs and then run the city without worry of poverty. The fact that boroughs could eliminate an entire concern of the game made it feel empty. The map of London, while nice looking, really wasn't that important to the game. Since boroughs could be bought adjacent to any player's borough and the map was then fixed, it was more eye candy than a functional part of the game. The tableau building was excellent. Important decisions to make about the size of the tableau vs. the negative effects it would provide. In all, I'm glad I played the original but even more excited to try the second edition.

RATING: 8
Very low interaction, but I like that in multiplayer games with lots of card text. Saves all that time reading everyone's cards.
As usual for Martin Wallace, there's lots of interlocking systems here all geared to tight resources. You want to do everything at once, but are constrained by limited money, growing poverty, hand limits on cards and a limited number of actions through the game. What to pick? More boroughs? Get more cash first? But need to earn points and reduce poverty. perhaps set up for the end game cards....
Flows reasonably fast. Good fun and leaves you thinking about what you could have done differently.

RATING: 7
[2013-11-25 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
I've only played London in the two player configuration with the Ben & Zen - Luca variant (see end of comment) and I still feel that it runs a little too long.

All in all, I did enjoy the game, it's surprisingly thematic. I like the need to balance your VP and money needs versus poverty and debts. I still have to see, if I can run a larger city with ten or more stacks successfully. Until now, I had at most six stacks. Because of the poverty mechanism and the one-time only cards, the decision when to run your city is interesting. I'm looking forward to future plays but I'm still not completely sold on it.

VARIANT
-----------
Essential variant for two player game, otherwise two player game is too long/easy: Ben & Zen-Luca variant. Rule changes:
1. Every time you empty a full display, the player who triggered this adds a neutral building marker to any free borough, and draws and discards the appropriate amount of cards for that borough. (Alternative: do this whenever a player buys a borough - although then you'll only end up with 5 each).
2. No face up duplicate buildings allowed in a single player's city (not certain this is necessary, perhaps only apply to Omnibus?)
Alternative to try: start with 15 poverty points.

RATING: 8
Brilliant design! This game and The Speicherstadt have opened a new category of games in my collection: the "Euro-Filler". 

Both are thoroughbred euro's with an economic theme but they consist of micro-turns and either can be played in an hour with 3-4 players, and probably 40 minutes with 2. 

And both games are perfect distillations of the designers' work: Wallace and Feld in their respective nutshells. 

At game nights what gets played is determined, at least once per night on most nights, by duration, so I generally have one of the two with me.

Edit: Raised rating to 9. Can be punishing, especially when compared to the point salad crap that's now in fashion. I love that this game doesn't baby you. 

Here's s little personality quiz:
If you get buried by a game and can't dig your way out, do you a) Blame the designer and say it's broken, or b) Want to play again to try to get it right?

London is strictly for type 'b's...

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
London, by Martin Wallace, is a card game with a small board attached to it. I have had a mixed relationship with Martin Wallace’s games. I am a big fan of Age of Steam and a moderate fan of Brass, but I have not been able to quite get into his more recent releases, and have found myself repeatedly excited about the idea of his games, only to find myself disappointed in their implementation. I pre-ordered London hoping it would break that trend, only to find myself disappointed when it merely confirmed it, and established Martin Wallace firmly in my try (multiple times) before buy camp.

London is fairly straightforward mechanically, each turn you draw a card, and then either play as many cards as you want into your city, which allows you to run your city, buy a borough, which gives you victory points, reduces the poverty you accumulate when running your city, and lets you draw cards, run your city, which activates your card’s special abilities, or draw three cards from the deck.

Further twists are added by the inclusion of loans, which allow you to fund your purchase of cards and boroughs, and poverty points, which cause you to lose victory points at the end of the game depending on where your poverty is relative to everyone else.

The cards themselves add much of the variety the game possesses, but they in themselves are much, much less interesting then something like Race For the Galaxy, Glory to Rome, or Innovation. In fact, my general opinion of the game is that, while it is adequate, it is much less interesting than most of the other games out there, lacking the interesting and crazy card combinations of Glory to Rome or Race For the Galaxy, and the in-depth interaction of Glory to Rome or Innovation. It is just sort of there, existing as a slightly boring game that is taking up a largely unnecessary niche. I can’t recommend this one unless you really, really like card games. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
rules are simplistic but cards make it complicated. First game frustrating to get a handle on. Excellent strategy game but little player interaction.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players contribute to the re-building of London in the centuries following the Great Fire.  Card game, with a board.  Players take turns to draw a card, take an action, then onto the next player.  Actions are to build a city borough (costing you money, but giving you cards and victory points), play cards (onto the table into your beanfields, I mean building display), or run your beanfield.  Running your buildings mean paying any activation cost, earning a reward, and possibly flipping it over and out of the game - as well as earning some poverty points based on how well or how badly you've established yourself.  It's all about investment and return.  Nice game, quite simple, but still a bit of a weird beast - perhaps a little like San Juan in the card cost mechanic and building powers.  Tells a nice story, got me pleasantly wedged with some indecisive angst during the game, and the poverty points mechanics keep you honest.  Enjoyed it - perhaps a touch long and repetitive, and the board seems almost superfluous - but gives the game some nice context.  Fair bit of boredom in the four player game waiting for your turn, as interaction is low there is nothing to keep you engaged.  As a 2 player game, worth a look.

As much as I admire the game, I find it a bit mechanical and lacking in fun. part of the "draft stuff and build a strategy" style.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Themed around the rebuilding of the City of London, a deck of cards shared among all players is used to generate money and VPs. To play most cards requires discarding another card matching the colour, making it freely available to other players. The cards improve over three periods as the game progresses, changing the gameplay drastically. Money remains a tight resource the entire time, making it difficult to accomplish much without it. Players can invest money to take ownership over a number of districts in London, which help to alleviate the influx of poverty points acquired throughout the game. At the end players compare points from the districts, cards played and a comparison of poverty points to arrive at their final score. Very fun gameplay with plenty of excruciating decisions to make.

60-90m, best with 2-4 players. 5+ plays.

RATING: 6
London is in essence a tableau style card game.  I typically love this type of game and tend to try as many of them as possible.  This one includes a board.  I can't say that it shouldn't include one, but it does increase the cost of the game substantially compared to other card games.  Another noteworthy thing to mention about London compared to others of it's ilk, is that it's not a short affair.  This game is going to run about 30 - 45 minutes longer than similar games.

It's an easy game to learn.  On your turn, draw a card and do 1 of 4 possible actions: play cards, activate cards (aka running your city), buy land, or draw 3 cards.  There is a large balancing act in terms of getting money (there are also loans available), getting victory points, getting rid of poverty (negative VP at game's end if you do not have the least), as well as hand management.  There are many ways to do this.  You just have to find a way that's more optimal than your opponents.

Each card is designated by phase a, b, or c and the deck is comprised of phase a cards first, then b, then c.  While the game is enjoyable I'm hoping the game doesn't become scripted due to the composition of the layered cards and the fact that most of the cards in the deck simply revolve around vp, money, poverty or some combination thereof. Two-player games should use variants to prevent a dual omnibus strategy, and to tighten up some other aspects of the design.

While this is a good game, the length ended up being the biggest turn off for me compared to other tableau building games.

RATING: 9
Described by some as the most "euro" game of Wallace, like an advanced version of Saint Petersburg or even San Juan.  Seems best as a 3 player game - as two player game it gets too easy at the end with money/poverty points.  Outstanding in every way!  
See my comprehensive pictorial review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/677333

Essential variant for two player game, otherwise two player game is too long/easy: [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/621605/the-ben-luca-2-player-variant]Ben & Zen-Luca variant[/url].  Rule changes:
1. No face up duplicate buildings allowed in a single player's city.
2. Every time you empty a full display, the player who triggered this adds a neutral building marker to any free borough, following the normal adjacency rules, and draws and discards the appropriate amount of cards for that borough. (Draw and discard four cards if all boroughs are already claimed.)

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A simple yet still interesting take on the complex card game genre. The card-play mechanic of discard a (color) card to play a (color) card leads to very satisfying hand management decisions, and the way the cards interact differently in terms of VPs, money, and poverty keeps the game relatively tense. There’s a nice pull on adding cards to your city for those tasty benefits vs trying to control your sprawl as it leads to poverty, and I find a small vs large city both viable strategies which is nice. Poverty is the best feature of the game IMO because of the way it adds another constraint while forcing you to keep an eye on your opponents. 

It's an OK game with a moderately interesting challenge in terms of playing cards vs controlling your city size but the decisions aren't painful enough and the interaction isn't strong enough to provide the kind of experience that I think about between plays. On the other hand, it’s become my go-to gateway game for someone I think might enjoy the hobby or at least wants to try one of these games they hear me talk about.

RATING: 7.5
Theme almost present :
- the deck is made of 3 chronological decks that respect the history of the rebuilding of London
- poverty, represented by the cards, against which we must fight throughout the game (the more you have cards in hand and neighborhoods in the city, the more you must deal with poverty)

Three issues for me :

- on 2 players configuration, it is easy enough to get rid of the poverty

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
London is a combo-building card game. There is a board as well but it is pretty much unnecessary. Interaction is very low. There are a few card effects that also affect other players but those effects aren't dramatic, and each player chooses his actions solely for himself (in contrast to, say, Race for the Galaxy). As there are only a couple of basic effects, and usually many cards implementing them, card knowledge isn't essential, and you're not as dependent on specific cards.

Of course, that also means that plays won't be as varied over time. The main challenge in London is finding the right point in time to switch from build-up (i.e. generating cash and/or cards which are necessary to power up your engine) to VP generation.

RATING: 8
London has a super smart card mechanic, connected strongly to the theme. I really love how all the parts come together. This is a wonderful game of balance and tough decisions. The learning curve is a bit steeper than expected, but you will get it before the game is through. This is more of a gamer's game than one would think given the paucity of the rules. It is not complicated but a lighter gamer may not have the easiest time keeping track of all the effects and repercussions of running their city.  There are familiar mechanics of gaining money to buy stuff and earning VPs, but London really plays like no other City Building / Economic game. I think what it does best is forcing the player to adapt to the cards in their hand. Each player needs to know what to sacrifice, when to sacrifice, when to reap and when to sow. I really do enjoy the decisions this game forces you to make. London still has a hard time escaping its rather repetitive "gain VP and gain money to do stuff" structure, but that point is far out-shined by everything else.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A card driven economic engine game based on building the city of London. The top 8 cards of the discard pile are displayed and can be drawn as if they were the top of the deck. When playing a building card, you need to discard a building of the same color, like Glory to Rome. Those two aspects help the randomness of the card draw and add a nice element of strategy to the game.

The board play wasn't very interesting, though a few cards gave bonuses to the location of your districts. The presence of the Take-That buildings seemed out of place. The opening plays seem a little scripted: unless you have a killer opening hand, populate a district for cards, taking a loan if necessary. Then, you need to build up some quick money in buildings and run the city. After that the game has more variability.

The longevity of the game will be the cards themselves. I could see this game gaining or losing my esteem based on how interesting the interactions remain.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another hit by Wallace. This is a light to medium weight game that has deck building as a core mechanic and adds a board/spatial element to the mix. I like how poverty works. You can have a lean machine (tableau with a few cards) or a huge powerful machine; one will not generate much but at the same time won't generate poverty cubes while the other will be punished for being so big. Each people's play style emerges here and it is so evident that I found it fascinating.

RATING: 8

My first impression of London is very, very positive.  This is his most streamlined and friendly game so far.

I am more impressed than ever after playing it for a third time.  It is a very simple game yet offers a good amount of strategy and planning.  The end game rushes up so quickly, but doesn't feel artificial.  This might end up being my favourite Wallace game of all.

Some concerns over there being too much of an optimal strategy.

RATING: 7.5
Use the Ben & Zen-Luca variant for 2 player games http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/621605/the-ben-luca-2-player-variant 
1) No face up duplicate buildings allowed in a single player's city display. Building something, flipping it, then building the same card again later is allowed.
2) When a player expends a card forcing a clearing of a row in the card display, that player places a neutral marker of a third colour in a city borough of their choice. The appropriate number of cards based on that borough are drawn from the deck and immediately discarded. 

Changes in upcoming reprint:

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
London is not a great game, but it is among the best and most replayable of the Treefrog games. I've already played it more times than any of the other Treefrog games - which often seem to be fun for one or two plays and then crash hard. I enjoy figuring out which cards I want in my city, and the wonderful draft/expend card display system helps make the decisions interesting. I don't think the two-player game works very well. Four is best, provided you have no slow players.

There seem to be two main approaches to the game. The first is to cycle through the stages of buying one or two boroughs, playing cards, and running your city. The other approach is to buy several boroughs right away, discarding most of the cards instead of playing them, then running your city repeatedly in the late stages of the game. The second approach will definitely win if it finds most of the best permanent, non-flipping cards: Omnibus, Omnibus, Lord's, British Museum (all from B deck) and Natural History Museum, Grand Hotel, and either Public Houses or Parliament (all from C deck). Unfortunately, this latter approach is robotic, ham-handed and uninteresting. London is a card game, so it doesn't bother me that favorable draws will sometimes (usually?) determine the winner. It does bother me, though, that one of the two major strategies circumvents all the interesting tactical parts of the game. I prefer to play with four players because this strategy is very weak with that number.

My second criticism of London is that the game system is too damped and constrained. Play falls into predictable patterns, with no opportunity to break loose. Your task is to identify and manipulate small efficiencies within the tightly scripted game flow. This scripted play really highlights the game's lack of depth. There isn't enough room to maneuver around the luck of the draw, and there isn't enough opportunity to outplay your opponents.

Some players will also criticize London for its very limited player interaction. For me this is a feature rather than a bug. But if you were disappointed with the level of interaction in games like Saint Petersburg, Race for the Galaxy, or 7 Wonders, then you will be similarly disappointed with London.

RATING: 9.5
The way in which the cards inter-relate with each other has given us plenty of options here on how to utilise these cards to form your Building Display and then you have to decide exactly when to Run the city. I have tried various strategies like taking out several loans in order to build the cards which have high VPs and then building over them, but I was caught out by not earning enough cash by the end game to pay off all my loans. Another strategy was to build cards which reduced Poverty Points but I didn't buy enough land to offset the generation of 'new' Poverty points whenever I ran my city !!

Martin W has given us a great challenge here to get the balance right to produce a winning score. Most of the folk I have played with have enjoyed it, the only 'moan' being about the game end trigger. I have produced my own one-page summary for this which I use to highlight to new players the need to plan ahead for the end game scoring so that they maximise their opportunities to influence their final score.

RATING: 7.5
London has fairly simple and straightforward rules.  It is easy to learn, teach and play, but it is very difficult to play well.  Players are forced to try new strategies each game as the cards will appear in a different order each time.  This keeps the game fresh, challenging and intriguing.  Although Wallace's games aren't traditionally prone to generating expansions, this could be an exception, as it is easy to envision additional building cards being made available.  That would certainly be welcome, as it will continue to add to the options and difficulties in mastering the game.  High marks to Wallace for designing a game with an intriguing historical backdrop that is easy to play, yet extremely challenging and enticing.

RATING: 7
A simple game that does a lot with what it has. There are essentially only two currencies: money and cards. This means the game can move at a fair pace as at any time it isn't too challenging to assess in what way you want to change money into points. Poverty is a minor niggle, but it is effectively a form of points, just with some complications. The theme comes through impressively from relatively simple card interactions: paying the police to move poverty along feels thematic, as does sending your paupers through the shipyards for cash. A good light financial game that gives a decent heft of game in under an hour.

RATING: 8.5
Gives me the same feeling and satisfaction than swimming breast stroke:

1) Glide - Enjoy the money made in last cycle, buy some areas and fill your hand for a new cycle. The longer the glide the better it feels.
2) Pull - Play your full hand of cards in preparation of the next phase, load yourself like a coiled spring.
3) Kick - Run the city and release all that energy to start a new, loooong glide towards the goal, hopefully making distance to your competiton. And like in swimming, if your pull is not completely done when you start the kick you'll get punished (by poverty in this case) and it will affect your position in the contest.

Great game, where the enjoyment really comes from optimizing the technique and flowing seamlesly from one phase to another.

RATING: 4
After one play, I can see some of the strategies, especially some of the card combos.  This is a balancing game, where you can't ignore money or poverty or number of stacks.  Poverty markers can be overcome with the right cards, but will you get one?

My second play was a 2-player game with the Ben-Zen-Luca variant.  Even with the variant burning through cards, it took too long.  I think this game is rife with the opportunity for analysis paralysis.  There are too many choices with all the different cards in hand, similar to Race for the Galaxy or Glory To Rome.  Do I build this card on a stack for its powers, and if I do, what do I discard to the Card Display (which then becomes available to the other players)?

This isn't nearly as good a game as Brass or Age of Steam, which don't inspire so much paralysis based on the luck of the draw.

Update: What I said above remains true, but the shine is fading off this one pretty quickly. The problem is downtime. RATING: 10
This is seriously a great game, with unique card-driven mechanics and Martin Wallace pedigree. It is what San Juan is to Puerto Rico, as London is to Brass. The flavor is there, with just the right amount of abstraction to make the game speedy and fun to play. San Juan and London loosely share similar city building mechanics, where cards can be several things. However, London keeps the card morphing simple, leaving out the money and poverty as tangible items and not as cards. Also, London has a board component, giving an additional tangible element lacking in San Juan; players loosely engage in an area-control/VP grab through boroughs, but these areas are critically tied to the relative efficiency of running the cities represented by cards.

The game has a sizable footprint, for being a "card game", but it is manageable. The components are good, although it is highly recommended sleeving the cards, which get a lot of use.

Although there was concern that the game was broken, based on various comments on BGG, this doesn't seem to be the case. If players are alert, no single player will get away with the majority of the boroughs. The game offers what looks like a number of different strategies, each with a distinct feel. Players will likely pick the strategy based on their starting hand, so each game will be unique. There are also a number of tactical card plays available and waiting to be discovered. Agonizing choices, tight economy, and a fascinating balancing act between money versus poverty. As players get familiar with the cards, there may be a risk of scripted play, but this too doesn't seem to be the case. However, there are a number of cards where we wonder "why?", as they seem to serve no useful purpose. I suppose with more plays, the usefulness of these cards and how they interact will become apparent leading to even newer strategies.

This is one of those games that just simply "clicks". So, it is no surprise that it is in the BGG Top 100. A good game that is becoming great, that plays in 90 minutes, scales wonderfully from 2 to 4, with 3 possibly being the sweet spot. 2-player is more like chess, with more turns, more money, and more options. With 4-player, the game could end quickly, if players rifle through the deck for juicy cards. Other than that, I don't see any flaws. Quite popular seeing lots of play. Initial rating a '9', with very likely bump to '10' if re-playability isn't an issue.

Update (09/11/11): There been more than 2 recorded plays, I estimate 20 by now. This is brilliant, brilliant, brilliant. My friends who love Brass love this as well. It scratches the Brass itch in a different, unique, and unexpected way using cards in a novel fashion. Upping to a '10'.

Get. This. Now. 

Update (09/24/11): 3-player clearly gives the best experience in terms of length and tension. 2-player seems to be too open, while the 4-player speeds up the game pace, making specific cards less valuable, and resulting in a less satisfying overall city building experience. A minor quibble--the game can suffer from weak-right-player binding, where due to unfamiliarity with the cards the right-hand player may discard some juicy cards to the benefit of the player to his/her left. It is critical to discern what cards to throw away, as much as what cards to keep--"one man's trash is another man's treasure."

Update (10/10/11): 2-player is yet another totally different experience. There is little or no downtime, so play is quick, yet the game length isn't shortened, since the deck has the same number of cards. Since both players will be able to get more boroughs, this encourages even larger city footprints (number of card stacks), for more dramatic and exciting running of the cities. This becomes a perfect "battle of the wits" game, that doesn't feel deterministic and dry like chess. It isn't quite two-player solitaire either, as there is some interaction through the card discards. The game has many layers of strategy and tactics that may be tried and uncovered over a long period of repeated plays. Highly recommended for two or three players.

Update (11/26/11): A comment was made in that there is some element of luck in the draw of the cards. Yes, if you're lucky enough to draw two omnibuses, that can be devastating to others. Drawing University of London, and drawing Parliament later is surely a death-blow. They can be defeated, but rarely, and surely not in a 2-player game. At a surface glance, it would seem that the game boils down to how many new cards you can draw in search of these premium cards; I don't think it is a problem yet, as it is a legitimate, viable strategy. I'll have to observe more and see if this is a long-term issue.

Update (12/27/11): It seems there is yet another surge of comments that the game is "broken"; I am still not convinced this is the case. The game is about rebuilding London, so this requires buying boroughs. Duh! It is not a question of IF there is an optimal "strategy" of the necessity of buying boroughs; you simply cannot win without going into debt and buying boroughs. I strongly believe that this was the original intention of the designer. If one can get past this misconception, the game is then primarily about the pace, frequency, tempo, and finesse of buying land versus playing cards versus running the city. Ideally, each player will employ different tactics, different building combinations, different card stack sizes (I don't think you want to deviate too far from a one-to-one correspondence of stack-to-borough), different debt sizes, and so on to respond to what is discarded and what is played by the other players, as well as adapt to what is drawn by the player themselves. Also, preparing for the end game is sort of an art form in of itself.

Normally, I dislike card games that require memorization of a lot of cards to play well, such as Glory to Rome or Innovation. Yes, the first game may be a miserable learning experience (like mine was), but I believe the cards' worth are relatively easy to evaluate within a game or two. Perhaps that is why I didn't mind London's card variety at all. 

As for comments about this game being multi-player solitaire, I don't think you can play London in a vacuum. It definitely requires monitoring of the other players' building tableau and discards, which reminds me of "Riich" Mah Jong . In this sense, it has far more "interaction" than Empire Builder, Dominion, or Merchant of Venus. 

Update (04/14/12) 33 plays: In a 4-player game, the player to the right of me "gifted" me with 2x omnibuses. I then proceeded to get 7x boroughs. However, others got the University of London and Parliament, while Public Houses was trashed. I went overboard on getting boroughs, while rapidly depleting the deck. The right-hand player laid a "trap" by giving me those boroughs, and quickly killing the game, leaving me with 9x cards in hand (I managed to whittle down to 4x cards). 3x unpaid loans killed me despite top scores in boroughs and cards. Don't get too greedy, and always monitor how quickly the game can end.

Update (09/29/2013) 44 plays: Still a GREAT game. A number of observations--
1) The relative value and significance of building cards, money generated, VP range, and pace will be vastly different between 2P, 3P, and 4P games.
2) University of London, Parliament, and Public Houses are key buildings, since they allow you to control the pace of the game. but do not necessarily guarantee a win.
3) Seemingly worthless cards can actually be worth a respectable amount of VP, if you consider present cost/total VP gained (taking into account the absolute net value of money, VP, and poverty combined).
4) Small footprint cities (4-6 stacks) usually do not generate enough cash, nor do they have enough inherent VP (brown hex points) accumulated.
5) Large footprint cities (10-12+ stacks) generate lots of cash, have enough inherent VP, but must have a way to control poverty. Duh!
6) Card discards into the common display can make or break the game of your left-handed opponent.
7) Do not be afraid to take loans. However, you must have plans to generate cash during the "C" deck to pay them back. Duh!
8) Perma-buildings (cards that do not flip) are very nice, but don't get too attached to them.
9) Just because you have a lot of boroughs, or a lot of stacks doesn't mean you're going to win.
10) Cards that give you VP chits throughout the game can be significant.
11) Do not be afraid of too much poverty early in the game, but have plans to be relatively even or have less poverty than your opponents by end game. Duh!

RATING: 7.1
Martin Wallace se sale de su línea habitual al hacer un juego de combos con cartas con tablero, claro y nada intrincado. A algunos les suena a Race for the Galaxy con menos azar y con más gestión que Dominion. Emplea mecanismos conocidos en otros de sus juegos como la gestión de un "recurso negativo" (agua en Tinners, descontento en Struggle... pobreza en London) o la escasez de dinero en Brass y sus préstamos se reproducen aquí en plan "Cofidis" (pide prestados 10 para devolver ¡15!). Su principal mecánica es el descarte como combustible para poner en marcha otras cartas, algo poco novedoso, pero yo no tengo ningún juego así. Pros: sencillo que no simplista para ser de Wallace. Dominion 2.0 ¡sin expansiones! en el que hay más libertad de movimientos y más combos CONTRAS: cartas únicas que hay que conocer muy bien para saber los combos. Poca interacción. Sobreproducido. Precio elevado. Algo de texto de cartas en inglés.

Baja de 8.9 a 7.8 en Oct. 2011 tras la última partida en la que volví a ganar usando la misma estrategia.

RATING: 9
After my first play I thought London was a clever game with some interesting mechanics.  After my second play I really started to appreciate the depth of the game.  Despite having a fairly simple rule set and gameplay, London is a shockingly deep and strategic game.  The interplay of money, land, poverty, and your cards leads to some really interesting inflection points (Do I go deeply into debt to build up on land and reduce my poverty?  Do I rapidly expand my City Display to rake in the cash, poverty be damned?), and it's very easy to imagine any one of those strategies being successful.  There is a strong element of multiplayer solitaire but for some reason that doesn't bother me much in this case, perhaps because of the ever-changing Card Display that may give you opportunities to snag great cards a la Thurn & Taxis.  London is a great option when you want a 1 1/2-2 hour game with a lot of meat to it.

RATING: 7
London is a very interesting, unique card driven game with lots of theme. There are many interesting choices to be made--when to buy more city districts, when to play cards that provide cash or victory points or reduce poverty. The mechanics work well. My first play was versus two players well experienced in the game so I was at a serious disadvantage--similar to a first time Race for the Galaxy player against experienced players. I enjoy the game and look forward to exploring it further. I like the way the cards are played. I feel like there's quite a bit of race for the galaxy influencing the design--for example, paying to build cards by discarding other cards--yet it is very different in several ways. I recommend London, especially if you like Euro card games and or an appreciation of the city.

Its not terrible at all, its just barely mediocre though imho and A Few Acres its a massively improved/upgraded version (with the caveats its only for 2, and the Halifax Hiccup...)

RATING: 7
2014-07-07

Initial Rating: 7.0 (July 2014)

Wallace did it again. London is a good card game and it has a lot of potential to go even higher in my personal ratings. Truth to be told, I didn’t want to play London (not sure why) but I’m glad I did. London is (relatively) easy to teach but has a lot of meaty and interesting decisions each turn (which cards to build, how many buildings in a row, which card do I use to pay, should I get a loan? Should I build in the city? When should I run the city and get poverty?). 

Money is very tight in this game, so knowing when you should stop building or take a loan to build is not a trivial decision.

The game board is very pretty and functional. The poverty concept is great and it functions as another layer you have to consider when thinking about your moves.

When I played London, I felt I was playing Race for the Galaxy. I meant that in the sense that you have to choose which cards to play and which cards to spend, these cards can be then used by your opponents and you again, very tense, very hard. It is great!

I kind of like the theme, but I didn’t feel it much in the game.

I think it is best played with 3 players, with more the downtime begins to be too much for me.

In conclusion, at the present I don’t have anything bad to say about this game. I want to play more soon and I wonder why I haven’t heard much about it.

One of 2010´s favorites.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Uses a number of Martin's familiar devices: the card deck's split into sequential sections a la Liberté, you need to manage 'bad resource' in a similar fashion to Struggle of Empires (poverty in London, unrest in SoE) and money's tight (as it is in pretty much all of Martin's games).

However, this is all applied to  a card game engine similar in someways to San Juan in that you'll have to discard half the cards you draw simply as fuel to allow you to build the other half into your city. Other nice mechanisms include using scarce (at least in the early game) money to purchase sections of London which give you VPs and additional cards, the decision on how often you run your city (do it often and the poverty points could kill you, but leave it late and you'll run out of money), and the bonus points to be had from building the Tube.

And it looks good too - very nice card art.

Some have complained that there are a lot of cards to memorise (the majority of cards are unique) but the cards are largely self explanatory and are generally variations on a small number of themes. It's simpler than many of Martin's games without being simplistic, and doesn't have any of the counter-intuitive mechanisms that can extend the learning curve on some of his games (e.g. Ottomans and Hapsburgs in God's Playground),
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Buying land is an important part:
- it reduces poverty points at each 'run city' and gives VP at the end of the game.
- if you need cards, instead of taking 3 cards, consider buying land. Not only it gives you more cards (up to 5) but also VP.

Try to play with a lot of cards. Like this you have more choice to play better cards. If however you got too many, no problem, just discard them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A card driven version of Amun Re designed by Martin Wallace.  The randomness of the cards seemed to cause a lopsided victory, but I think repeat plays would mitigate that somewhat.

RATING: 7
I like this a lot. Imagine playing Race for the Galaxy, except you get to remake your entire tableau every few turns. The tradeoff is, that the powers on the cards are not nearly as varied as in Race, mostly various combinations of get money or victory points or deal with poverty cubes. So compared to Race it's a tradeoff of less varied cards for the ability to remake the interaction of the cards and see more of them, more often.

The mechanisms interlock very well- drawing a card every turn makes it impossible to build a city where every card doesn't require flipping and play mindlessly to the end- the cards keep coming, which means poverty points are increasing, and you have to add to your city to deal with them. Very nice. The game forces you to adapt as the cards come, just as London was forced to deal with the increasing pressures of a large, industrializing city.

I also love the theme and think the theme is well expressed. But then, I'm working on a PhD in economic history, so if anyone on BGG was predisposed to like this theme, I suppose I was (and that was part of the reason I tried to get a copy). Street lights and sewers lowered the burden of poverty! Monuments are nice (VP) but did little towards running the city. 

This makes me want to try A Few Acres of Snow for another Martin Wallace card-driven board game.

RATING: N/A
Preordered.

From my Essen Watch List (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/58505/johans-essen-2010-watch-list/page/1):

I read the rules last night, and am really tempted to place a preorder. Somewhat simplified it's essentially a card game by the looks of it, but with some board play as well. It seems fairly straightforward: draw a card, then either play a card (possibly paying for it), run your city or draw 3 more cards. 'Running' a city means activating some or all of the cards you've previously played, and the more cards you have the more poverty cubes you end up getting (which gives negative points at the end). One interesting twist is that you can play cards on top of other cards, where only the stack counts towards the poverty cubes, not all the cards in the stack (while cards in the stack still gives points, this does prevent activating the overbuilt cards). The board play seems to amount to allowing you to subsequently play certain other cards, as well as giving an option for more points by having a presence on the board. An added bonus for me is that it works on two players out of the box.

RATING: 6
Good solid game, some interesting ideas here (like most Martin Wallace games).  Played alot when the game first came out (Dec. 2010 - Jan. 2011) and probably would have rated it higher (a 7 or 8) then, but these days it doesn't seem to hit the table that much.  I think that, once you are familiar with all the cards, the replay value just isn't there.  There seems to be one kind of set way to play, which is to play cards early for money, then spend it to build districts to keep a check on your poverty, repeat for a few cycles, rinse and dry -- going for points, of course, later in the game.  There are more tactical nuances to the game, but that's about it.

Not as good as Automobile, IMHO.

RATING: 8.5
Martin Wallace game that is at its heart a card game, with a small spatial element added.
