=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Martin Wallace has built an impressive reputation on unapologetically inelegant and convoluted games. Some are glorious; some are dreadful. Increasingly, his influence is felt elsewhere in the modern design space: Vinhos would be this year's prime example.

London is a stark departure from all that, an easy-to-learn, tableau-style card game that answers the challenge laid down by San Juan, Race for the Galaxy, and Glory to Rome, as well as newcomers Gosu, 51st State, and Innovation. A turn is simple: either play new cards, activate old cards, or draw more cards (mostly by claiming regions on the board). The deck is simple: three suits with mostly straightforward functions. Brown cards generate cash; pink cards generate victory points, blue cards mostly help reduce poverty. The cost to play a new card is consistent across the deck and throughout each phase of the game: simply discard a card of that color to the board display. Only the cost to activate cards varies much. Interaction between players is gentle, mild, and indirect, probably moreso than any other game I've ever played.

London has been described in my game group as "relaxing," and really, if you know anything about Wallace's previous titles, that tells you all you need to know about what a strange experience this is. It's "fine," it "works," it's "playable," etc., but I won't be going out of my way to explore it further. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
This is a deeper game, but the rules aren't overly complex and it can be played in under two hours. Select the cards from the stack or ones that other players have discarded face up. Build your economy by playing cards. Don't build too many or have too many in your hand when you do or you go into poverty. Buying sections of the city helps prevent the poverty. I found it usually better to take from the random stack since players tend to discard the weakest cards. I like this game.

[b]Verdict: Keeper[/b]

[b]Two player "Ben-Luca" variant:[/b]
1. No face up duplicate buildings allowed in a single player's city display. Building something, flipping it, then building the same card again later is allowed. 
2. When a player expends a card forcing a clearing of a row in the card display, that player places a neutral marker of a third colour in a city borough of their choice. The appropriate number of cards based on that borough are drawn from the deck and immediately discarded.

RATING: 6
The combo aspect is not as strong as I expected on first play which makes London more accessible for me. It's rather a game of timing money vs VP acquisition, relative positioning with regard to poverty tokens and hand management. While the two-player game is less tight it's probably my favourite due to the reduced downtime.
---
Martin Wallace does a card combo game. Not exactly, what I was looking for but enough folks will enjoy it. London is another prove that card combo building doesn't work for me. It's more enjoyable than Friese's parallely released take at this genre, Fürstenfeld, and I would play London again. But as usual I don't have a clue why my combos don't work as efficiently as others. I don't have the experience of Magic players in immediately recognizing good combinations and I don't have the interest in learning all the cards to play better. Too many games with better return on investment out there. London is a bit too long for the fun provided. After half the deck I wouldn't have minded an immediate end. I'm glad that I wasn't the only clueless around as until the final scoring it wasn't clear to any of us who'll win. Or what to do better next time.
As with all Wallace games, here's the part on quirky design decisions: The game doesn't scale between players. With the full complement of four players the burroughs will have been bought in mid-game. Players have less actions to choose from, the tension drops. Tinners' Trail had the same problem and therefore was best with three players.

A good chunk of the game is spent fine tuning your hand, discarding stuff and waiting for others to discard stuff.  This feels completely against the spirit of the game, but I don't see any way around it.

RATING: 9
London reminds me somewhat of Liberté with its age decks and card displays. This time the displays have been refined quite a bit and the gameplay is smoother and more about the cards than in Liberté. Board is really just an extension to card play. For a Martin Wallace game I see London as fast flowing and even quick. The theme is nice and well implemented and there's more interaction than you can see at first. Luck of card drawing may bother some, but the nature of the game is purely tactical and it's about timing your actions right and grabbing the opportunities. The starting player unfortunately has a bit of an advantage and there is no balancing mechanism.

RATING: 9

That being said, the poverty mechanism still leads to a rich game, and the powers' simplicity makes the game very fast-paced.

RATING: 6
I've heard a lot of people bellyache about this game, and I don't for the life of me understand why.

Most of the time you end up trying to turn a sow's ear into a silk purse. Loans are particularly punishing (Hello, Mister Wallace!), and the Very Special Lose Veeps at the End Condition (poverty) can be nasty if you don't really work at unimpoverishing your Dickensian masses. 

I like how there's a real sense of history and growth here. Before playing, it was hard to imagine how Martin Wallace would do with a predominantly card-based design, but this is exactly what I would've pictured, and it works very well. I can imagine burning out on it after eight or ten plays, once you've seen every combo and can ferret out the good ones, but until then I probably won't turn down a game if it's on offer.

UPDATE: I find this to be too long for two, even with variants. It's fun but a clunky one.

[b]Players considerations[/b]: 
Played with two and it works great, three and four I'm sure is just as balanced because the deck doesn't actually run out faster because more cards can go in the display, giving everyone more choices to take cards from.

[b]My thoughts[/b]:
Love how the game is balanced between spending/saving money, playing/drawing cards, and building buildings/activating cards. The poverty level is intimidating but always seems to decrease steadily throughout the game, but if you spend all your time on correcting that you'll be broke by the end and have lots of loans you cannot pay off. A game of scarcity, which I am indeed a fan of (just like Notre Dame).

RATING: 9
First, let me state that I think Martin Wallace has hit upon another really wonderful board/card game system.  I can see this design system becoming as popular as Age of Steam (which I'm a big fan of).  I do think this game, published as is, does need a little more fine tuning (or at least to my taste) and needs more work in balancing cards/buildings relative to the rest of the cards in the deck.  In my opinion, there should also be cards relative to or scaled to the number of players.

I think this is a really good card drafting and card combination (CCG-like) game where players are utilizing cards to make businesses/buildings in London while buying land to help develop London.  The keys are card management and running your city and maintaining a level of poverty that maximizes your victory points strategy. There's lots of choices here with several strategies to adapt and lots of card combinations to pursue.  

This seems to be a good for two and three players.  The game, nor the rules, allow for scaling for two versus three versus four players, which I would have greatly preferred.  With 3 or 4, I wished there were more cards added that were labeled for use/added during 3 or 4 players.  Also, I firmly believe that the "University of London" card is simply too powerful (and under-priced) and only one-of-a-kind.  I think it should just be ignored from the game or replaced as a gray card or shuffled into Deck C.

I do really hope that there will be expansion decks available in the future.  This system can work wonderfully with different maps as well with a card themes adjusted to the map according (for example, expansions could be New York, Los Angeles, Hong Kong, Tokyo, Paris, Munich, and so on).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial impression quite favorable after one play.

LIKES:

* Cards have very interesting combinations in how they work together.
* The way poverty interacts with your cards stacks/cards in hand brings an interesting piece that you must manage well in the game.
* Money is tight in the end game - be careful not to spend all your money and find out you don't have enough to pay back your loan (which results in -7 points per loan!).  
* Purchase districts in the city to help manage the poverty level and earn VPs on the board and draw cards.
* I also enjoy the tension between buying districts and drawing cards, playing city cards, and as mentioned before the interaction with poverty.

DISLIKE:

* The end game can become a bit stale as it drags on around the table until someone feels like they are at a good place to either end the game or have to end it.  Be sure you are prepared to deal with the possible consequences of the end game!
* I have significant concerns about the replayability of this game.  I'm also not certain if there is much variability to how one can play this game successfully, but time will tell.

RATING: 7
My wife and I tend to like tableau-building card games, such as games like [thing=8217][/thing], [thing=28143][/thing], and [thing=19857][/thing].  It's fun to try and build up your area as well as trying to make use of all the special actions on the cards.  As a result, we've been interested in [thing=65781][/thing] for a while, as it seemed like an evolution on these types of games.  Luckily, our Secret Santa gifted us a copy of this for the new year and we eagerly tried it out.

I was an immediate fan of the artwork.  The map design and the card art just felt very classy.  I liked the introduction of the poverty mechanic as a thematic way of subtly limiting your tableau.  The fact that most cards could only be used once before being flipped over was interesting as well, so you had to pick the right timing to run your city.  The card display, reminiscent of [thing=9217][/thing], felt like it could provide some options for player interaction---what cards do you leave there?  When do you try to pick them up?

We ended up using the [thread=621605]Ben-Luca variation[/thread] recommended on the forums and got started.  This helped make the game feel tighter for two players.  However, there are some design oversights that annoyed us.  It would have been [i]much[/i] simpler to have a score track instead of going through and counting all of your cards and boroughs at the end.  Most of the points are open anyway, so that would have saved a lot of time.  I wish there was an easier way to sort the cards back to the phase 1, 2, and 3 decks afterwards as well.  In terms of gameplay, the timing of when to run your city didn't feel as interesting as I had hoped.  Since you turn over, or inactivate, many of your cards when you run the city, the game feels less like a steady engine builder and feels more like trying to time different waves of action.  In addition, the map felt fairly irrelevant---although there are a few cards that make use of the map, it didn't feel very critical to the game.  The card display didn't feel very interactive---in fact, my wife commented that this felt too "multiplayer solitaire" even for her.

In the end, although there were some new ideas here, there was really nothing in [thing=65781][/thing] that we couldn't get out of something like [thing=28143][/thing], especially in a fraction of the time.  Still, it's a game that I probably admire more than I like...

RATING: 7
In the wake of Race for the Galaxy and Dominion, resource management card games are all the rage and London is Martin Wallace’s entry into the genre. Nominally about the reconstruction of London after the great fire of 1666, London mixes things up a bit by having a gameboard featuring a map of city districts that the players can claim, rather than being a straight-up card game.

Nevertheless, the heart of the game lies in the cards. You play buildings from your which do nothing until you “run your city,” at which point all your face up cards do that thing they do. Those things include generating money, generating victory points, preventing poverty (which is damaging, but only at the end of the game) plus a few other special powers here and there. Some cards require an additional price to activate – although the most common price of discarding a card from your hand can be quite beneficial – and most are one-use-only, so you flip them face down after you activate them.

The catch is that there is tension between wanting to play as many buildings as possible and playing them in small numbers. Cards are played into stacks, and when you run your city you can only activate the top card in each stack (provided it is face up). You can build over other cards keeping things under control and you don’t want to create stacks willy-nilly because each stack generates a poverty point (whether you activate its top card or not) when you run your city. You also generate one poverty for each card you have in hand when you run your city, though, so hoarding cards for your next go round has a cost as well.

At the end of the game you tote up your VPs from buildings constructed (including those buried in the depths of your stacks), VP chits earned, the city districts you have claimed and money on hand, with a penalty for poverty which depends on how much worse you are than the player who best kept their poverty under control.

Overall I enjoyed playing London, but I’m not sure yet whether it has legs. There are some issues with card balance (already there seems to be a consensus that one player getting both Omnibuses is a gamebreaker, something I experienced in my game) and I don’t think the low poverty strategy is as viable as one would hope because of a flood of strong poverty-control buildings in the late stages of the game. On a personal taste front, I wish there were more cards that stayed around for more than one run to add a bit more strategy (versus working with what you’ve got), possibly including cards that you could pay an additional price (or accept diminished returns from) to keep in play. Also, I’d want a significant reduction in playing time once you’re familiar with the game (three of the four players in my game were first-timers). It feels like a 60 minute game, but the learning curve makes your first game likely to run to 90+ minutes.

I am definitely up for playing more to see how some of those “what ifs” resolve, though. If enough things fall on the right side of the line, London promises to be a rewarding mildly-strategic resource management card game, in the same vein as Saint Petersburg if a bit heavier.

RATING: 8
In my mind this is Wallace's take on RftG; not an entirely bad thing.

As a whole I have no issues with the design itself. The cards work well. The cards, while basic, blend together seamlessly with no "dead" ones in the mix. I even like the discard area and the player driven timer that it represents.

The game is, however, completely unintuitive and dare I say totally counter intuitive. This in combination with the classic unforgiving Wallace design sense in which there are usually few chances to catch up created a very frustrating and skewed-scoring first play.

Willing to play at least one more time to decide how disappointed I really am.

Update:

Play number two with two major changes:

1 - It's amazing how much better the game plays when you have the rules correct and play more than one card per "play cards" action.

2 - Everyone took loans. Another massive improvement and removed many barriers in the game's economy.

Those being said the game was wonderful, tense, highly competitive, and thoroughly enjoyable.

My rating rises from a 6 to an 8.

RATING: 7.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Martin Wallace is a prolific designer with a strong fan base and famous for his relatively complex (at least as far as finding the optimum stategy is concerned) historical and economic board games. London has some key elements of Wallace’s designs, such as tight economic system (once again a loan is inevitable), but on the other hand it is a very simple game to learn and play in comparison to what we are used to.

In my turn, I draw one card (from the display or common deck) and then I have one action to choose between play cards from my hand, draw 3 cards, run city or buy land. At the end of my turn I have to discard down to 9 cards.

Cards represent buildings and locations of London and provides us with money and VPs and also affect poverty, but some of them also have special abilities. When I play cards, I place them in fron of me in stacks and for each card played I have to place a card of the same color on the display, which is therefore available from any player to draw it later. Each time I take the “Run city” action, cards that I have played already in front of me are being activated (most of them once per game) and then I take poverty cubes for each stack of cards I have and for each card in my hand minus one for each property I own in London map. Poverty is important because can cost a lot of VPs at the end of the game. When I “buy land”, I pay the cost depicted on the board, draw as many cards as indicated on this location and mark it with my token. These properties are important because decrease poverty, worth VPs and are related to many cards.

The game ends as far as the deck is depleted, followed by one last turn and players count VPs (cards played with VP on them, VP tokens accumulated previously by run city actions, 1 VP per 3 money, VPs from properties, minus poverty penalty and 7 VP per unpaid loan).

So, the game flows well, it is an enjoyable game, but where is the catch? There is a standard strategy that you have to follow: Take loans, buy as many land as you can early in order to limit your poverty. Then later you can run city without taking poverty. If a player follows this strategy, then others should follow if they want to have chances for victory. You draw many cards this way that most of them have to be discarded. I don’t like this because many cards (about 40% – 50% of deck)are discarded without being played. But all in all is still a good game. Maybe too mainstream for Wallace’ fans (count me in) but London will appeal casual players who run away when Wallace’s games hit the table. If you like drafting, probably you will like this game.

(+)
Easy to learn
Plays well with any number of players
You are being familiar with London’s locations

(-)
Standard strategy to victory
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This could be a great game. Unfortunately it's not. The main problem is that loans are very cheap. Thus, you can manipulate this "bug" by getting a loan(s) and buy land. The other players have to follow the same strategy and after that there isn't much of a game left. 

So, in order to make it work as a game, you absolutely need house rules. I have tried a lot of them - some work better than others:
#1. Loans are more expensive both in money and VPs
#2. Remove some building tokens depending on the number of players (e.g. for 2 players, each player has 8 tokens, for 3 players 7 tokens, for 4 players 5 tokens)
#3. You can't buy land, if you start your turn with 9 cards OR you can't get a loan, if you start your turn with 9 cards
#4. You can't have two same cards simultaneously in your city (e.g. 2 omnibus)
#5. Getting a loan is 1 action
#6. You can buy a specific number of boroughs in each step (A-2, B-3 and C-5)
#7. For each card you have to discard above your hand limit when you buy a borough, you take 1 poverty cube
#8. Each time you buy a borough and you have an outstanding loan, you must pay some interest (e.g. 5 pounds). If you take a new loan, you must first pay off your previous one. 

I prefer #6, #7 and #8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
I've only played this game two player, but it felt broken to us. The person who won was the one who got the money production cards early (thus allowing them to produce enough money to pay to run/build more expensive cards and buy boroughs). That could simply be called a dominant strategy, until you factor in that this is a card game where luck of the draw has a huge influence on who gets those cards. That made the game feel very unsatisfying for us, especially considering how long it is. We weren't interested in ever playing it again.

RATING: 7
London is another strongly themed game from Martin Wallace. Players take turns in either playing cards (which represent buildings/ institutions) to their building display, or claim land in a borough or 'run' their city (by activating their played cards). Like most MW games there are lots of difficult decisions to make. Players have to generate cash, earn victory points, claim land and minimise the amount of poverty that builds up. This is essentially a card game with simple rules (no more complex than Tinner's Trail).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
London is Martin Wallace's take on the efficiency engine card game genre dominated by greats like Race for the Galaxy, Through the Ages, Glory to Rome, and the relatively unknown 51st State / The New Era and now forgotten Saint Petersburg. It's relatively straightforward as a member of this genre, being built mostly around the income / VP tradeoff central to these sorts of games and around Martin Wallace's trademarked focus on loans. It reuses bits and pieces of the pay-to-play mechanism and consumption phase [even the same "goods" icon!] from Race. Some cards give you infrastructure, others give you weird special powers, still others let you start burning the midnight oil in your engine to crank out the points. Lather, rinse, repeat and you're all done.

What it does very interestingly is place a limits-to-growth system on your engine-building in an organic way. The system is kind of like the Happiness mechanism in Through The Ages, but is much more explicitly linked to the engine-building. The central idea is that every time you "turn the crank", you effectively incur a cost / penalty based on how many cards make up your engine. To allow you to manage this, you're able to "stack" cards on top of each other; so, as you gain access to more efficient cube-churners, you can replace your old technology, etc. Thematically, the cost is dubbed "poverty" and the more you accumulate during the game, the more penalty points you will have to pay at the end of the game based on an increasing scale.

There are, of course, cards that give you the ability to reduce your recurring penalty or eliminate previously earned poverty. Effectively, the entire poverty system is one of managing administrative / overhead / fixed expenses, and it works quite well without the clunkiness of something like Through The Ages' maintenance phase or the tacked-on feeling of the more usual "feed your workers" phase in most larger efficiency engine board games. The penalty is directly tied to your efficiency, allowing you to make interesting opportunity cost decisions; you can clearly see what adding a new stack will do to your costs, and judge that against the expected gain to see if you'll net a profit or a loss. It's a decision type that is usually absent from games like this; in Race, if you have a good card and can pay the start-up fee, you just play it.

Where the game kind of falls short, and feels most strange, is that most of the cards you play down only activate one time and then effectively leave your tableau [they get flipped over, in reverse-Brass fashion]. You're left with the overhead, but not the output. This means you have to keep struggling to piece together new parts of the engine to manage the cost structure you've set up. It feels very weird, and is almost wholly unlike any other game in this genre I can think of. 51st State / New Era comes closest in that there is a hard 3-use limit on VP-producing cards, but that is nowhere near as game-defining. Martin Wallace seems to enjoy this thematic representation of growth by removing from play the source of the economic gain once used [see the new income structure in Age of Industry or the oft-confusing thematic interpretation of deliveries in Age of Steam], but it makes his games feel herky-jerky and this is no exception.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I had convinced myself that I didn't need London, considering it was out of print and didn't seem all that innovative. Surprisingly, it turned out to be a perfect example of Wallace doing what he does best, which is presenting a simple rule set with a ton of depth. I tend to love games of his like this, such as Steam and Brass, where learning the game isn't difficult, but doing well is an entirely different story.

In a lot of ways, this feels like Wallace doing his own version of Race for the Galaxy/San Juan. The map element here is one I like, though the Poverty element is one that constantly keeps players on their toes. It's slightly reminiscent of corruption in other games like Lords of Waterdeep, but here it's far more punishing and difficult to get rid of. I really like the tenseness that it creates.

My initial research was correct; there isn't anything all that innovative here. The design itself, however, is one that I very much enjoy. It's a game of building a tableau of cards, but there's a certain balance and rhythm you have to maintain in order to do well. I know this game gets mixed reviews from people, but I believe it's one of Martin Wallace's best works.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
One is attempting to rebuild London from the fire of 1666, in this card driven game. One either plays cards, activates cards, buy boroughs or draw three cards as their action. After you activate cards, some get flipped over, never to be used again, while some stay permanent. 

Money is very tight, and you can take loans when you need money. You can also accumulate poverty tokens from the cards or activating cards, so one also has to worry about reducing their poverty tokens, as at the end of the game, they reduce your score.

This is Martin Wallace's best game to date. The rules are simple, and the stacking of cards is a pretty neat mechanic. I really enjoy this game, and want to play this a ton more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A real niche-filler. Somewhere between being long and political and being short and uncontrollable, lies London. It's a glorious exercise with just one variable more than you would like to keep under control and - of course - particularly apt to denizens of the city itself. Not RftG-esque, as others have claimed, the key management and timing strategies make it closer to Dominion, or even Carcassonne. The artwork is sheer class and the game wouldn't be the same without it.

Further Thoughts: the big issue starting to arise with this is the amount of downtime. You could go walk the dog and cook dinner in the amount of time it takes for three opponents all to run their cities. Above this, there's a bit of a question over whether the game is just playing itself. Downgraded from 9 to 8.

Further, Further Thoughts: Down again, to 7.5. The game is totally immersive, and I love that. But we've seen it all now, and in under ten plays. Shame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
This game is quite good - its a relatively casual puzzle game, where you are balancing growth and stability (poverty);  like many games, you play cards in front of you as a tableau - these cards are buildings that usually have some kind of special ability and/or point value;  ideally, you want to build things that work well with each other, or provide benefits which will help you grow further;

however, the more buildings you have, the more expensive it is to run your city, so you have to manage growth carefully;  also, you are penalized for keeping a large hand size, but too small a hand size presents you with little options - so timing when or how to play your cards is critical; if you play or activate your cards at the wrong time, it will be inefficient, to say the least

Some other features:

1.  there is little player interaction

2.  there is a bit of luck, but it seems especially manageable;  I never felt the cards were screwing me over

3.  game weight is about a 2.5 out of 5.0, maybe even a little lower

4.  the game will easily take the recommended 90 mins

5.  great as a two player game, especially if using the variant proposed here on BGG

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*卡牌照A/B/C分類洗勻疊起，A在最上面
*每個人拿一個顏色的建築板塊跟5元跟5個黑色方塊(5黑方等於一黑圓片)，每人抽六張卡，隨機起始
*遊戲照順位輪流進行，每次輪到玩家時先抽一張牌(牌庫或開牌區)，在行動4選1，之後手牌超過9張要棄到圖板上
>圖板上的放牌區先放上面列，擺滿在放下面，在擺滿移除上面列，下面列上移，之後再擺新的，每列只使用人數+2格，遊戲隨時缺錢可借貸拿借貸板塊跟10元，最後要付15元否則扣分
1.出卡 可出多張卡，下方要一列三格的表示能出到面前(沒有的一次性使用後棄掉)，可蓋掉之前的但是不能是當回合的(正面或背面皆可)，出到面前的每張都要支付一張同顏色的放到圖板放牌區，如果右上有數字代表要付的錢
2.運作牌面卡牌 可啟動多張面前的牌，最下方左邊代表啟動花費(放牌到圖板/付錢)，中間代表好處(錢/移方塊/分數)，左邊表示使用後是否要翻面，啟動可依任意順序，結算完後，算手牌張數跟面前疊數，總和扣掉圖板上的建築數拿或棄黑色方塊
>如果啟動地鐵卡則放兩個地鐵板塊在地圖上空的有建築板塊(不用自己的)的區域上，第一個放到場上的要放到城市或相鄰格，之後放的則一定要相鄰場上的一個地鐵板塊，如果過橋要額外付3元
3.買地  放一個建築板塊到一個空的區域，第一個買的要放在紅色名字的區域，之後則要跟其他建築板塊(不用你的)相鄰，過河的要有橋才算相鄰，支付該格左邊數字金額，抽中間數字的牌，遊戲最後有右邊數字的分數
4.抽三張卡 沒牌抽就盡量抽，如果沒牌還是可選此動作當作跳過
*抽完牌庫的玩家結束後，每個其他玩家在一回合遊戲結束，每個10元貸款要支付15元，然後每張手牌拿一顆黑色方塊
*每3元1分，每個建築板塊所在區域拿右邊分數，有地鐵板塊額外加兩分，每張打出來的卡(不管正反面是否被蓋掉)拿左下角的分數，加總之前拿的分數，每個沒付的貸款-7分，所有玩家比黑色方塊最少玩家多的黑色方塊數查圖板右上表扣分，平手比黑方塊少的>建築板塊放最多的>卡牌單張分數最高的
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.5
Will no longer play the game without rule tweaks due to 'broken' strategy.  Even then it is just dull.  

Everything is simply just points.  You take a loan for 10 and pay it back for 15, or it is worth -7VP at the end.  Since 3 cash is 1 point, then if you take a loan and dont pay it back, it is simply -3.7VP. If you do pay it back it is -1.7VP.

Poverty seemed interesting at first except for the fact that this has no effect on the game at all, except it gives you -VP at the end of game.  

So, when you look at buildings, some of them give a mix of cash, poverty and VP, but in the end, all of that just easily translates to points.

Some of the buildings are just clearly better than others.  Some are broken.  If you get 2xOmnibus's you will proabably win.

But it is the fact that the building boroughs and discarding cards wins every game, that everyone needs to do it, meaning that all the A cards might aswell be blank.

Very much a rushed out game, must have missed some proper playtesting and just an all round disappointment.  Probably the worst purchase I made in 2011.  Sorry Martin.

RATING: 9.5
My favorite game from Martin Wallace. A card game but not a deck-building game, London is a semi-heavy strategy game that fits different elements of property-buying, card-drafting, and gathering victory points in one swift package. The game plays pretty fast with any number of players and it plays differently every time. Do I eliminate poverty or try to accumulate as much money and victory points? Or do I try a little bit from both? My only issue with this game is the quality of the currency: cheap, little tiddlywink plastic pieces of shit. Sigh. But I can't fault a game for the cheapness of its components. Or can I? Not to worry, Paulo and I just bought the Brass deluxe coins to go with London. Phew! Problem solved!

RATING: 7

RATING: 6
You pick up cards, you build cards Uruk style (discard a colour to build a card in that colour to your tableau) and then activate all those card powers to get money, get rid of negative-VP cubes (which are acquired every time you activate your cards), earn VP's, etc. Money can buy you more cards via buying a borough on the board, and some cards have placement costs, so you spend it to make it. In short, it's a pretty standard tech buying tableau game with a number of one-use-only cards which means you re-build your tech sets, providing some intra-game variety. All good and fine ... except for the killer downtime while each player on their turn evaluates whether to pick up from the display of up to 10 cards, all with different powers, or pick up blind. Then working through their options in their hand. Which can be interesting on your turn as you evaluate and move between different combinations and strategies, but urggh when it's not your turn. After a couple of plays, the promise of interesting strategies also seems to diminish - you want to make money, convert it into VPs via cards or boroughs, get your poverty cubes down. The cards you use may differ slightly on how you do this, but their effect is the same. And the cards just keep coming each turn, and you need to play them out to keep your poverty down, so the tactical card management process offers little variety. Lastly, the game doesn't provide much thematic pull and you play in your own little world with little player interaction or tension. It's for tech card game lovers only.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
London is an engine-building card game.  Each turn, you have the choice of either playing building cards to improve your engine, or actually running the engine.  Running your engine may give you points, money, or other benefits, but it will also create "poverty" depending on the number of cards you've played and the cards you still have in hand.  Poverty is worth big negatives at the end, but only relative to player with the least.  There are lots of strategic decisions about how big you want to make your engine, and how many times you want to actually run it, while trying to manage your money too.  Over many plays, I'm starting to find that opening hand luck is very important; the player who gets the most big-money cards in their opening hand seems to have a distinct advantage in the game.

(5+ plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This is an intense and very deep city builder. The way it works is, each turn you draw a card. Then you decide what you want to do on your turn. You can build a building, by paying money and placing your house on an empty spot. This draws you cards. You can play cards, but each card you play you have to discard a card of the same color to the available pool of cards that other players can grab from. Your third option is to activate the cards you played on previous turns. When you do this, you get the cool benefits of your cards, but many are one time use, and it also generates poverty cubes for you based on the number of card "stacks" you have plus cards in hand, minus number of cities built on the board. At the end of the game, being stuck with a lot of poverty cubes punishes your score significantly, so you have this fascinating tension throughout your turns while you try to balance efficiency vs safety.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
If you look at my profile you'll see that two of my gaming loves are card-combos and Wallace-style economic games, so combining the two sounded like a dream ticket. But the absolute killer for London is the lack of interaction. I've played three times now and felt like I barely looked up from my hand and tableau at the other players. Unlike Race and Glory to Rome, the type of action you choose doesn't really affect anyone else, and waiting for someone to run a 10-card city has the same feeling as drumming your fingers while a Village Idiot finishes their turn in Dominion.

It's also less thematically integrated than most Wallace designs, and feels like it falls into a set pattern of optimisation. Buy boroughs to fill your hand with cards, play as many cards as possible (using the display to make matching pairs), run your city while your hand is empty to make money to buy more boroughs and so on. And the board, while prettier than the usual Wallace, is almost an afterthought, which could be replaced by more cards or a list of payouts without losing much.

The poverty system is the highlight, and it does make for some interesting decisions about whether to have large and infrequent or small and frequent city runs. But the cards themselves aren't interesting enough, some are strictly better than others, it's very hard to tell who's winning or why, and it's twice as long as it needs to be, with a particularly painful endgame. Of the card combo games, it feels most like Innovation (because of the stacks, and the play/activate separation) but with less interesting cards and all the interaction taken out.

RATING: 6
Decent, but not great (at least as a 2p game with the Ben-Luca variant). 

Tableau building game with a bit of randomness and some interesting ideas (particularly the poverty, which serves as a punishment mechanic that forces you to plan for the best moment to activate the tableau). There's not much of an engine building here, since most of the cards have a one-time effect. Still, there are many things to think about - playing each card requires discarding a card of the same color, which can be later taken by one of your opponents. It has a very nice flow and is rather enjoyable, but for me, it doesn't have any WOW! factor. Also, the ending can be a bit awkward, when noone draws the cards to finish the game, instead activating the city again and again.

Was ok while it lasted, but for us it didn't have enough staying power. After a dozen of plays it started to feel a bit samey, even with randomness of the draw.

RATING: 7
Ranked after two plays -- both two-player, using the Ben-Luca variant. My fiancee's new favorite game. Lots of interesting decisions -- feels very dynamic, although the rules are simple and there isn't too much variability --particularly among boroughs. I'm giving it an 8 for what I can best describe as subtle, elegant mechanics. The only downside has been that it drags a bit, and there isn't a whole lot of interaction. As with Innovation, I'm looking forward to this game improving with time, as we get to know the cards more.

Edit: downgrading to a 7. Game length has become a very important consideration for us as of late, and as several other reviewers have correctly pointed out, this game -- at 90+ minutes -- doesn't pack a lot of punch per minute. Even with the Ben-Luca variant (which I consider a must), the game drags far too long, and the whole boroughs game is completely boring. We ended up just quitting our last game about 2/3 of the way through.

If it were a shorter game, I would be more excited about mastering the subtleties of the card interactions, but as it stands, I'd much rather play Innovation, Seasons, Dominion, Race for the Galaxy, or even San Juan, to get my card-game fix.

RATING: 3
Note: Just started rating games, rating may be subject to change.

Played twice, once as a 2er and then added a 3rd player. First game was pretty interesting - a lot of cards to read, but you had to make painful decisions about which ones to keep. I was surprised at my second game how obvious the choices felt, and how few decisions I felt I was making. Some cards are obviously better than others. Hospital feels broken - combos very well with a number of cards. In terms of gameplay, it really feels optimal to first buy a district to draw cards/score points, then build your city with your new hand, then run your city to make money so you can buy another district and repeat (except at end game, when you've run out of district and want to gear up for many runs). City size seems optimal around 6 - enough that you can build with most of your hand and keep a couple good cards around between builds. Really, this game does not feel like it has legs to stand up to too many playings. I'm willing to play it at least once more to confirm this, and maybe another time to ensure there isn't much variance in strategy, because two plays isn't necessarily enough... but I think ultimately under good play the game is going to boil down to who draws better cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.4
:) Remarkable depth and strategic possibilities for what basically is a card game with pretty simple rules. The mechanics in this title are original (you build a city by playing cards in a number of decks (you decide how many). 'Running' your city gains points, money and different abilities, but also tends to create poverty which you need to keep under control). I love the engine building part and the flexibility the game offers me in terms of creating a small city or a big one. There is also a strong focus on hand management: most of the time you need to pay a card in order to play another and you have to figure out ways in order to dispose of certain cards which you don't want.

:) I wouldn't call the game mechanics thematically grounded ('paying' (discarding) St Pauls Cathedral in order to build Nelson's column doesn't really make any sense, does it?), but still there is thematic flavor to be found in all the different monuments and buildings found in London. You can revisit your citytrip playing London :) What's more, many cards tell their own real story in the effects they cause: you can educate initially useless paupers for a price in order so they can help you erect other buildings - or you can dispose of them in your prison, reducing poverty ([i]in that sense, I wouldn't call this game really politically correct, but hey - thematically, it makes sense[/i]).

:) Money can be really tight, creating an engine whichs generates money is all the more satisfying because of it.

:) There is quite some excitement going on as the game nears its end. It can be difficult to predict when the last rounds gets triggered, and being able to run your engine a final time often makes all the difference of the world point-wise

:soblue: The mechanics are original, rather elegant and offer flexibility to try different strategies, but they are not without flaw. There is an annoying aspect of 'luck of the draw' which plays an important role in an otherwise strategic game. Especially when the game nears its end, the game can put certain players up with an impossible hand which can leave them with a ton of penalty points without offering them any way out.

:soblue: In a pretty futile attemt to create player interaction, a few cards allow for negative player interaction (stealing some money, giving poverty to other players, etc). I feel this is uninspired, annoying and doesn't match the flow of the game

:soblue: Some downtime can occur when players start collecting many cards and need to puzzle out which cards to keep and which to discard. However, overall, downtime is limited and reasonable in London.

RATING: 6.5
Pros:
Delightfully themed, very engaging game. There is an ebb and flow to the game that needs to be grasped before longterm strategies reveal themselves. It's also interesting how you have to adjust your tactics to the stage of the game (beginning, middle, and end). The winner is often determined by very narrow margins, which makes for a tense gaming experience. 

Cons:
Not good with only two players. Luck of the draw plays a prominent role in determining the outcome between two equally matched players. Also, the boroughs are central to any winning strategy, so with only two players the early game is dominated by a borough arms race. However, the game presents such an interesting puzzle that, win or lose, the journey is entertaining. You have to balance your strategy and hope that the next draw will be good enough to make up for lost opportunities. I just wish the game offered more ways to mitigate luck and regain lost ground. 

- The 2nd Edition of London addresses all of the cons I've listed here. The 2nd Edition is a masterpiece.

Also it seems that there is not much of a strategic element to it. You basically take the most out of the cards you get. The length is a plus and I will definitely try it again but overall it didn't fulfill my expectations for it.

RATING: 7
Wallace does the card-laying-tableau-building thingy, and does it well. Surprisingly light, with a game length, load of vp calculating at game end, and dour art reminiscent of the old Ladybird books, that don't quite match with the lightness; so in all, a slightly odd beast. UI am guessing that at one stage before being simplified, the poverty cubes and other things actually went down on the map; the cubes and the board itself seem to be slightly out-of-place relicts of earlier incarnations. 

I think it's likely that I prefer the game that exists in a parallel universe somewhere in which the board and actual location of developments (and poverty) have greater significance - but I do find I enjoy this version all the same. 

As is typical with Mr Wallace, there are lots of very neat little ways that historical peculiarities and detail are worked into simple, abstracted mechanics. 

Enjoyed 4 player the most so far (feels very urgent) - 2 player is excellent, but poverty doesn't quite seem to work (maybe we need to use strategies that take on loads of pov early on).

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very little player interaction in this game that should max out at three players due to downtime. Rules are simple, but there's a fair bit of information on the cards to read through and lots of fiddly gathering and paying of cards, cubes, money chits and VP counters. Downtime is just a huge killer here. Because you take a new card on a building turn (sometimes more with special cards), you often need time to adjust your plans while building because money can be very tight (which is a good thing).

The game will reward further play as you begin to understand how easy it is to acquire money in the early cards, but how you'll need that money by the C deck when there's a lot of victory points to be bought and very little further money coming in.

The game also plays very differently with different player numbers. Poverty points are a non issue in a two player game as both players have plentiful access to poverty point reducing cards and many burroughs on the board. With four players you really notice the fewer number of turns and how much more difficult it is to reduce your poverty. With three it's something in the middle, but you've got enough turns for some control.

I'm not a fan of the endgame. The game seems to creep to a conclusion with little tension. I'd have preferred a seeded end card to add some drama and reduce the scheming.

All in all it's an engaging and simple game for exactly three players (because two players can chat about the weather while the other is selecting cards and calculating their money), but there's not much depth to it. There's a few different strategies to try out with building stacks and whatnot, however I can't help but feel that for the same feel you'd be better off playing Innovation with double the angst and half the clutter in half the time.

On further reflection I'd sum it up as: London is a wonderful game for three or four players as long as they are experienced fast players who limit the downtime.

----------------

RATING: 7

RATING: 7
Treefrog Edition.

Tentative rating for now.

Wallace's take on a RftG & Dominion with San Juan elements. PLayers are building the city of London with their cards. Draw cards, play it, activate it for money, VP, effects. There's another sub-game where players buy boroughs on the board. This helps give cards and reduce poverty. Poverty is a negative VP element that players need to manage. It goes up when you are trying to expand your tableau of cards too quickly or with too many cards left in hand.

The thematic link to gameplay is actually VERY good. You do feel like you're laying out the city and trying to manage the elements that go into it. Building sewers helps reduce poverty for example. Clever.

But jury is still out because of several negatives:
- too much down time in a 4 player game. It's essentially multiplayer solitaire.
- possibility of a 'broken' sure-win strategy. See this thread:http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/592475/can-someone-please-counter-this-for-me

Shame as the game can be fun.

RATING: 8.5
I love playing London! But... There is a part of this game that bugs me a bit. Really, I love almost every thing about it: the card play, the awesome card stack mechanic, the poverty, the Wallace loan system, etc. 

But the map just really does not integrate that well with the game. Purchasing the buildings works as a mechanic, but it just doesn't seem to fit. In our games, everyone generally buys up all the buildings (regions on the map) in the beginning, because - well, you have to. If you let the other players get them, you'll be screwed big time. 

Having said that, I always want to play this and the issue mentioned just fades away into the background shortly after I start playing. 

As for the two player - that took some work, but after eliminating some cards and tweaking a rule, it's now quite good. I have even devised a two player version where I don't even use the map at all - the Burroughs are listed on a piece of paper with all their characteristics, bonuses and restrictions, and thats really all you need. In the end - a solid 8.5 in my book. 

Love the art style too -

RATING: 8
Unlike any other Wallace game. This is a card game that feels a little like Innovation (you are playing cards (paying with other cards), then using those cards to generate points and/or money) and Dominion (don't discard every turn, but you only use most cards once -- your card display turns over sort of like Dominion hands do, but in slow motion over several turns). Also like Race for the Galaxy: you can keep your head down and do your own thing, but you'll likely do better if you pay attention to what others are doing, and try to counter them. Despite all the differences, you still have a few Wallace standards: loans, which you cannot pay off till the game ends, and poverty penalty points, which cost you VP. 

Looking forward to playing this more! I could see my rating of this go up, especially if it becomes a system -- with expansion maps and rules -- rather than just a game.


--- Ben-Zen-Luca 2-Player Variant ---
1) No face up duplicate buildings allowed in a single player's city display. Building something, flipping it, then building the same card again later is allowed.

2) When a player expends a card forcing a clearing of a row in the card display, that player places a neutral marker of a third colour in a city borough of their choice. The appropriate number of cards based on that borough are drawn from the deck and immediately discarded.

--- Larry Levy Solo Variant ---
1. You take your turn
2. Discard the top 3 cards from the deck
3. Add the next 3 cards to the display
4. Take your next turn and repeat
5. You're limited to buying only 5 boroughs. You can buy whichever ones you wish. Don't bother placing opposing markers, just limit yourself to 5 borough purchases.
6. Use the full 10 card display

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
A card game about one of the most interesting cities of the world but with no relevant theme element IMO. The set is in London but it could easily be in any other city of the world.
It has some nice mechanics such as the way players run their city or deal with the poverty points but the real decisions tend to be too much obvious for my tastes. 
As a disclamer I should say that I don't like card games in particular. That said, London has the ability to make players play cards as a single important decision to take during the game: which cards should I pick up and discard and which should I play in my city. The rest of the game actions, the real actions their selves are too much obvious: Players build boroughs to get cards, play cards and run city. Get cards, play cards and run city. There's no point in taking a different than obvious decision because it's too much obvious not to take it, sort of speaking.

I like games that have important decisions to take and not obvious channels to follow. London seems too much like an obvious path in which players with the most experience/patience seem to do better most of the time. I prefer a game that explores some risk and decision making towards the flow of the game itself and towards the final analysis rather than a previous conceived decision taking (dull) game.

RATING: 7.5
London, Wallace's 'card game', or so I was lead to believe.  Sure the cards are important and the board a little less so, but this is almost as much a card game as Brass.  ...And this is no Brass.  Take that as a slight, but let's be honest, Not much compares favorably to Brass, so let us not bury London yet. 

It is a solid design and gets to the meat quickly.  You can pound out a game of this in 45 minutes and it ends up being a satisfying if not memorably experience.  I always enjoy how Wallace finds a way to add some sort of action efficiency in his designs.  In London, you have to balance money and poverty with victory points; but it is not the best balance that wins, it's who accomplishes these things in a more efficient manner.

RATING: 6.5
There are some things in this game that I like, and some that I hate.

I don't like: complicated poverty system and penalizing effects of it; some cards which once obtained by one player give him ultimate advantage over others (thus, randomization in this game sucks, very powerful cards are scarce and while these may not be a key to the victory, without them you may be in troubles); loans, which are another tool of punishment; downtimes; finally, turns and actions, which can be take in any order, usually work in one rhythm only.

I like the fact that the game forces me to find new strategies every time I play, to find some way to balance poverty, number of cards and building strategy.

In sum, there are some things that are raising my ranking for this game, but in overall it is not worthy enough.

RATING: 7
Another card game, but not a standard one. You can build as many stacks with your cards as you want, but the more stacks, the more poverty. You don`t want poverty - minus points at the end of the game (just like in 20th Century).

You can use the most cards only once, so you can`t play a one combo the whole game. You need some luck, to draw the cards that you need. 

The interaction is on a very low level (discarding cards, that other players can take, and a few "bad cards"). 

London is different than other card games, but after one game i don`t see many ways. I think like in 20th century, you need to "recycle" the poverty, but to do that, you need the right cards. You must build houses in London (points and many new cards), to build you need money. To have money, you must activate cards, that brings money (luck to draw this cards when you need them).

Maybe another games will show more strategies, than it will be more interesting, not so luck dependend and will have replaybility.

RATING: 10
This game has a board and pieces you place on the board, but in reality it's just a card game; the board play is a very minor part of the proceedings. Not that that's a bad thing ... in fact, though I still have many new titles to play, I think it's likely this will emerge as my favorite game from the Essen 2010 crop. Players have an amazingly diverse number of options in regards to how they choose to play (conservative, with few cards stacks and generating few poverty points; sprawling, with a productive engine but lots of poverty; spendthrift, taking lots of loans and banking lots of points during the game but paying for it later; thrifty, with things slower to develop but no loans to repay), but any of these stragegies can bring victory. London is fairly simple and easy to teach but has some real depth. Martin Wallace continues to impress ...

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Falling a bit in my estimation after half a dozen plays.  This is a card game along the lines of San Juan/Race for the Galaxy/Glory to Rome with some typical Wallace twists are thrown into this mix:  loans of course, but also the poverty points that are continually dogging you (reminiscent of loss cubes in Automobile or water in Tinners' Trail).  I like the aforementioned games and much of Wallace's offereings, so I was very enthusiastic about this at first.
My concern now is that there isn't really room to pursue very differentiated strategies:  no player will want to fall behind on boroughs, so all should end with about the same number.  Because everyone is doing roughy the same thing, the cards tend to just be plain more or less powerful as opposed to being more or less useful depending on your strategy.  So the outcome becomes a question of who gets the greatest number of the really good cards (Hospital, Omnibus, Underground, Whitehall and maybe a few others) rather than who adapts a strategy to his cards the best.
The two player game seems both pointless (because there is no real pressure around poverty) and borderline broken (because the power of the Omnibus is magnified with only 2 players).  I'm willing to give it a few more shots with 4 to see if there is enough room for differentiation there.  But my fear is that this ends up as an interesting and well themed game that is cool for a few plays but ultimately lacks staying power.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
[u]Required variant:[/u] must be played disallowing 2 active Omnibuses in the same tableau. 

[u]Poverty in 2-player:[/u] to address the loss of poverty relevence in 2 player, each starts with 20 poverty (actually, next time I'll go to 25). Thanks to Bryan {Mr_Nuts] for the idea & reporting this successful variant.

I think with an experienced 3-player table, the poverty should also be increased to at least 10 as the starting set-up. It keeps poverty strategically meaningful. I do think inexperienced players needn't modify the starting poverty as tempo mistakes can really punish.

*

Getting good involves receptivity rather than forcing a path. The money & tempo management are clear & aren't overpowered, small gains that build in brief interludes that flip over & out of the game in an interesting core 'run city' mechanic. Leveraging the seemingly tiny differences in these cards & cashflow, and recognizing the situation that calls for one above the other, brings a heightened alertness & sustains replay. In 11 games I have started each with a different premise & direction, more an inquiry that bends & jukes through the game, and the different valuations of these same cards through the deck has surprised me. As well as the opportunities for denial, or upseting tempo - when they come. All of this makes for very many decisions in the game along with sustaining flow. 

[u]First plays:[/u] The dynamic between game elements is balanced, elastic, and variable. There is card management, money, raw VP’s, poverty reduction & attack, loans, borough placement on the map, an underground, a common pool used as resources and an alternate draw deck, and deck and endgame manipulation. The game does not reward any singular strategy over another; the elasticity will snap you back. With every powerful play, there is a cost, be it in one of the other variables or in tempo. Big money, for example, does not a win make.

RATING: 10
"London lies devastated after the Great Fire of 1666. This is your opportunity to build a new city on the ashes of the old. It is up to you how you employ the talents of the people of London to this end. Will you favour the business classes, who will earn you money? Or would you prefer to spend more money than you can rightly afford on grand monuments and sumptuous palaces? You must also deal with the problem of rising poverty and how to employ the many paupers of the city. Throughout the game you will be forced to make tough decisions. To achieve one aim you must sacrifice another, which may open an opportunity for a competitor."

Nearly 250 years of progress is glimpsed in this game. Famous buildings and monuments of the era as well as engineering developments such as streetlights are illustrated on the cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I am usually mildly enthusiastic about Wallace's designs. I think I have played many of his games from Age of Steam to La Strada. Liberte remains one of my favorite game from his collection and London fails to wrest the crown from Liberte. I have only played London once two player and while I do find it intriguing, I am a little bit suspicious about the replayability. First, it is true that player interaction is low. Each player has their own sandbox tableau to build, but the sandbox is quite large. You can have a variety of cards and strategies, but you don't really have to lift your head up to look at others to build your tableau other than when you buy land or properties from the main board. This is not a bad thing for gamers who like low confrontation/interaction sort of games. There is enough variation there to sustain several plays. 
     What I worry most from first blush is the cadence and rhythm of the game that feels samey from round to round. It is immediately clear that buying properties on the board is crucial to get several types of benefit: 1)reduce poverty points each time your run a city; 2) Score victory points; 3) Greatly increase hand size; 4) Earn income through card play. These positives make landgrabbing a common feature of the game. In a 2 player game, it felt that we were always going through the same cycles: 1)run city earn income; 2) Buy properties, draw cards; 3) play cards and the cycle repeats itself until end game. There was very little variation in that rhythm except that sometimes, two consecutive rounds maybe spent on buying properties or playing cards. After a while, it felt that my opponent and I were just going through the same cadence during each turn and it felt a little odd. Perhaps there is more variety and strategy than that, but I just don't think I see it that much. The next time I play the game, I will be tempted to go through the same exact cycle just because buying London properties seem to be such a huge part of the game.

RATING: 9
I was beginning to despair for the "discard cards to pay for other cards" mechanic.  I'd played the heavy-hitters in that genre: [gameid=8217], [gameid=28143], and [gameid=19857].  All had fallen flat for me.  But Martin Wallace has come through here, as he has created a game that is much more intriguing.  The simple addition of A, B, and C decks, and a shared tableau to which you discard your cards means that the game has much more staying power, a greater ability to plan beyond hoping to draw the right cards, and a synchronicity with the rest of the table that makes things very interesting.  Sure, the map is mostly extraneous, but it's a nice touch.  Short games don't get much better at blending theme and mechanics than this one.

RATING: 7.5
Draw cards, place them in your area, flip them over once you've taken the benefit, as most cards can only ever be used once. Cards in hand and the number of piles you have in your area give poverty, costing you points. That's the majority of the game right there. The board is a place to store the discarded cards, as you can draw from there rather than the deck. Also, you can purchase areas of the city, this gives you points, cards, and reduces your poverty. Didn't blow me away as much as I had thought it might. It was good, and I enjoyed it a lot, but it ran a bit long. Hard to judge perhaps because you don't know the cards at the beginning, so it was hard to tell which card to build vs what to discard, etc.

For 2 player games, I invented the [thread=621605]Ben-Luca 2 player variant[/thread], which has received lots of positive feedback from those who have tried it.

RATING: 5
Not Martin's best game. Played with two with the Ben-Luca variant. Without it, the game can become interminable if you cycle through the cards in the display instead of rapidly drawing new ones. The end condition is 'deck emptied'.

I was genuinely searching on the internet for house rules during our two-player game while my husband was taking his turn.

RATING: 6
For a Martin Wallace game, this is a pretty straightforward game, at least in the sense of how you play it.  The card combinations, and knowing when to time certain actions, is a little trickier, and the real heart of the game.  I've heard some comparisons between this game and Race for the Galaxy, and while I sort of get it (spending cards to play cards into a tableau that give you special abilities), I really don't see it at all.  The tension of the game is different, thanks to the way that the different actions benefit you, and trying to balance the money, the VPs, the buildings, and your poverty points is a much, much different experience than Race will ever give you.  It's certainly more accessible than other Wallace games, but that doesn't necessarily mean that it's any easier to play.

My first impression is that the game might devolve into a tactical game, which isn't a bad thing, but it's different than what I expected.  Additionally, because players can play as many cards as they want for one action, and then activate buildings in any order they wish for another, the min-maxing effect of the game leads to some AP-ness, and more down time than I would have expected for the game.  I think that 4 players is probably above my own threshold, but 2 players might not give the game enough variety.  Either way, I look forward to playing again.

-----

1 play: 4 players

RATING: 6
It's a very interesting economic game by Martin Wallace... or at least half of it.  

The povety mechanics and the tableau-building system work great, you have to carefully balance your development throughout the game. The only problem is that everything that happens on the map is rather dull - once all the boroughs are in place you can't really change or build anything there (apart from the underground, which is basically +2 VPs really). Also, you can really abuse the "poverty discount" strategy when buying a lot of boroughs early on in the game. I wish the spatial aspect was given more attention from the designer as it significantly  lowers the overall rating of an otherwise very good, clever card game (throw away the map and it's an instant 8/10).

/update (September 24, 2017): Great news, Martin Wallace learnt his lesson and got rid of the whole board in the second edition of the game. THAT's the game I want to try out now! I hope I'll be able to play it one day.

RATING: N/A
As influential Londoners we are trying to rebuild the city after the great fire in 1666. Color themed cards are drawn to flesh out your part of the city as stacks. To build a card you pay a card of its same color into a shared discard pool.  The size of your city increases your poverty level.  Investment in the surrounding boroughs offsets your poverty.  To run the city you activate the cards in your stacks.  Smooth, intuitive card game with some area control and economic sim. Fun balancing act.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This is my least favorite Martin Wallace game by a good stretch.  Mr. Wallace has his ups and downs, like other designers, but this one drops to the bottom of the barrel for me.

While building and running a city is an imaginative framework, London ends up being a pointless contribution to the Race from the Galaxy school of game design, in which players build their solitary, completely noninteractive tableau of cards and then squeeze money and victory points out of them.

What makes London significantly worse than those games (which I already dislike) is the following:

Players spend a lot of time (I mean, a LOT of time) staring into space waiting for their turn.

Through no effort of their own, players can randomly luck into cards (Huguenots, Jewish Immigrants) that bestow double-draw benefits.  There are also Pauper cards whose only purpose is to clog up players' hands with dead draws; these cards are, of course, also drawn at random.

Some cards are strictly worse than other cards, the only difference being that some cards are available for drafting later in the game due to the A/B/C deck structure.

In my first/last game of London, I found myself frequently hoping that the previous player would discard a certain color card so that I could play its color-mate sitting in my hand.  (Or, failing that, I was stuck hoping that I would draw the color myself.) The game's partial reliance on a hope-based strategy is unacceptable, and is certainly a hallmark of an underdeveloped game.

The board is barely necessary, as its only purpose is to keep track of action/VP areas purchased.  The game could be revised to eliminate the board entirely, which if nothing else would drive down the purchase price to a reasonable level.

The game is fatally flawed, in that the endgame trigger (when the last card is drawn) can incapacitate players who need to play their last few cards and run their cities/tableaus one final time to be competitive.  

In conclusion:

You should test the waters of this Thames disaster so that you don't inadvertently jump into the mouth of a river shark.  I value my lower extremities, and you should too.

RATING: 7
Very neat card management game. You are rebuilding London by playing cards in front of you. You then "run" your city and take advantage of the bonuses on the cards. The number of cards you have in play plus in your hand equals the number of poverty (negative points) you pick up. It is a balancing act between positive points, money and poverty. RATING: 6
Another decent game from Martin Wallace. It plays pretty quickly once you get the hang of things; short turns interspersed with some interesting decisions over when to run your city. I love the thematic integration, particularly how one good way of getting rid of the useless Pauper cards is to educate them with a School card. I have some reservations about the first part of the game being more or less scripted - some of that could have been absorbed into setup.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Painful. You're either dead broke or swimming in poverty. I really like the mechanics in this game: flipping cards to avoid broken, repetitive combos; the borough system granting VPs and cards while decreasing poverty; the poverty cube formula. Everything comes together great to create a well-oiled economic tableau driven card game. Fairly heavy for what is basically a card game.

That said, it is completely necessary to use the 2-player Ben-Luca variant. Works extremely well. The one problem I have in 2-player is that poverty can totally ruin you in scoring since you're only competing against one person. So really you need to prioritize being equal to or better than your opponent in poverty or you're pretty much cooked.

RATING: 1
Sadly, I've come to the conclusion that this game is broken after running through multiple solo plays (playing 2-4 hands by myself) using the "take loans to buy boroughs and cycle the deck" strategy.  I've tried various different approaches to counter the strategy, but it seems dominant and the only effective counter is to "do it too."  Then the game devolves into who can build the best hand of cards before being forced to actually build and run a city.  Too bad, because initially I had this rated quite highly and enjoyed it a good deal.  Now, it's going to sit on the shelf gathering dust.

Some people on here have worked up variants to try to eliminate this problem according to the forums.

RATING: 10
Delicioso juego con un impagable aire a Mary Poppins. Construye Londres a partir de sus cenizas: colegios, cafés, puertos , universidades, el parlamento, el Palacio de Buckingham, el alumbrado, etc... Todas las cartas bellamente ilustradas.
